of projective planes with a distinguished line, called the ‚Äúline at inÔ¨Ånity.‚Äù The morphisms
in each groupoid are bijections on both points and lines (preserving the distinguished line
in the case of projective planes) that preserve and reÔ¨Çect the incidence relation. The functor
Proj| ‚ÜíAfÔ¨Åne removes the line at inÔ¨Ånity and the points it contains. Explicitly describe an
inverse equivalence.
Exercise 1.5.ix. Show that any category that is equivalent to a locally small category is
locally small.
Exercise 1.5.x. Characterize the categories that are equivalent to discrete categories. A
category that is connected and essentially discrete is called chaotic.
Exercise 1.5.xi. Consider the functors Ab ‚ÜíGroup (inclusion), Ring ‚ÜíAb (forget-
ting the multiplication), (‚àí)√ó : Ring ‚ÜíGroup (taking the group of units), Ring ‚ÜíRng
(dropping the multiplicative unit), Field ‚ÜíRing (inclusion), and ModR ‚ÜíAb (forgetful).
Determine which functors are full, which are faithful, and which are essentially surjective.
Do any deÔ¨Åne an equivalence of categories? (Warning: A few of these questions conceal
research-level problems, but they can be fun to think about even if full solutions are hard
to come by.)
1.6. The art of the diagram chase
The diagrams incorporate a large amount of
information. Their use provides extensive savings
in space and in mental eÔ¨Äort. In the case of many
theorems, the setting up of the correct diagram is
the major part of the proof. We therefore urge that
the reader stop at the end of each theorem and
attempt to construct for himself the relevant
diagram before examining the one which is given
in the text. Once this is done, the subsequent
demonstration can be followed more readily; in
fact, the reader can usually supply it himself.
Samuel Eilenberg and Norman Steenrod,
Foundations of Algebraic Topology [ES52]
The proof of Theorem 1.5.9 used a technique called ‚Äúdiagram chasing,‚Äù also called
abstract nonsense, that we now consider more formally. A diagram is typically presented
informally as a directed graph of morphisms in a category. In this informal presentation,
the diagram commutes if any two paths of composable arrows in the directed graph with

The morphisms Œ∑: 1 ‚ÜíM in the case of topological monoids, Œ∑: Z ‚ÜíR in the case of
unital rings, and Œ∑: k ‚ÜíR in the case of k-algebras do no more and no less than specify
an element of M or R to serve as the multiplicative unit. We will introduce language to
describe the role played in each case by the topological space 1, the abelian group Z, and
the vector space k in Example 2.1.5(iii).
In the case of a topological monoid, the condition that ¬µ: M√óM ‚ÜíM is a morphism in
Top demands that the multiplication function is continuous; for instance, the circle S 1 ‚äÇC
deÔ¨Ånes a topological monoid with addition of angles.
For unital rings, the morphism
¬µ: R ‚äóZ R ‚ÜíR represents a bilinear homomorphism of abelian groups from R √ó R to R; in
particular, multiplication distributes over addition in R. The role of the tensor product in
the deÔ¨Ånition of a k-algebra is similar.
Let us now give precise meaning to the term ‚Äúdiagram.‚Äù
Definition 1.6.4. A diagram in a category C is a functor F : J ‚ÜíC whose domain, the
indexing category, is a small category.
A diagram is typically depicted by drawing the objects and morphisms in its image,
with the domain category left implicit, particularly in the case where the indexing category
J is a preorder, so that any two paths of composable arrows have a common composite.
Nonetheless, the indexing category J plays an important role. Functoriality requires that
any composition relations that hold in J must hold in the image of the diagram, which is
what it means to say that the directed graph deÔ¨Åned by the image of the diagram in C is
commutative. An immediate consequence of the form of our deÔ¨Ånition of a commutative
diagram is the following result.
Lemma 1.6.5. Functors preserve commutative diagrams.
Proof. A diagram in C is given by a functor F : J ‚ÜíC, whose domain is a small
category. Given any functor G: C ‚ÜíD, the composite GF : J ‚ÜíD deÔ¨Ånes the image of
the diagram in D.
‚ñ°

as formalized in DeÔ¨Ånition 1.6.4, and their informal directed graph presentations.

In certain special cases, commutativity of diagrams can be automatic. For instance, any
parallel sequences of composable morphisms in a preorder must have a common composite
precisely because any hom-set in a preorder has at most one element. Parallel sequences
of composable morphisms also necessarily have a common composite when the domain or
codomain objects have a certain special property:
Definition 1.6.14. An object i ‚ààC is initial if for every c ‚ààC there is a unique morphism
i ‚Üíc. Dually, an object t ‚ààC is terminal if for every c ‚ààC there is a unique morphism
c ‚Üít.
Example 1.6.15. Many of the categories of our acquaintance have initial and terminal
objects.
(i) The empty set is an initial object in Set and any singleton set is terminal.
(ii) In Top, the empty and singleton spaces are, respectively, initial and terminal.
(iii) In Set‚àó, any singleton set is both initial and terminal.
(iv) In ModR, the zero module is both initial and terminal. Similarly, the trivial group is
both initial and terminal in Group.
(v) The zero ring is the terminal ring. To identify an initial object, we must clarify what
sort of rings and ring homomorphisms are being considered. Recall that Ring denotes
the category of unital rings and ring homomorphisms that preserve the multiplicative
identity. This is a non-full subcategory of the larger category Rng of rings that do not
necessarily have a multiplicative identity and homomorphisms that need not preserve
one if it happens to exist. The integers deÔ¨Åne an initial object in Ring but not in Rng,
in which the zero ring is initial.
(vi) The category Field of Ô¨Åelds has neither initial nor terminal objects. Indeed, there are
no homomorphisms between Ô¨Åelds of diÔ¨Äerent characteristic.
(vii) The empty category deÔ¨Ånes an initial object in Cat, and the category 1 is terminal.

42
1. CATEGORIES, FUNCTORS, NATURAL TRANSFORMATIONS
(viii) An initial object in a preorder is a global minimal element, and a terminal object is a
global maximal element. These initial and terminal objects may or may not exist in
each particular instance.
Lemma 1.6.16. Let f1, . . . , fn and g1, . . . , gm be composable sequences of morphisms so
that the domain of f1 equals the domain of g1 and the codomain of fn equals the codomain
of gm. If this common codomain is a terminal object, or if this common domain is an initial
object, then fn ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ f1 = gm ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ g1.
Proof. The two dual statements are immediate consequences of the uniqueness part
of DeÔ¨Ånition 1.6.14.
‚ñ°
In certain cases, one can prove that a diagram commutes by appealing to ‚Äúelements‚Äù
of the objects. For instance, this is possible in any concrete category.
Definition 1.6.17. A concrete category is a category C equipped with a faithful functor
U : C ‚ÜíSet.
The functor U typically carries an object of C to its ‚Äúunderlying set.‚Äù The faithfulness
condition asserts that any parallel pair of morphisms f, g: c ‚áíc‚Ä≤ that induce the same
function U f = Ug between the underlying sets must be equal in C. The idea is that the
question of whether a map between the underlying sets of objects in a concrete category is a
map in the category is a condition (e.g., continuity). By contrast, the functor U : C ‚ÜíSet
is not faithful if the maps in C have extra structure that is not visible at the level of the
underlying sets (e.g., homotopy classes of maps [Fre04]).
Example 1.6.18. Each category listed in Example 1.1.3 is concrete, although care must
be taken in the cases of Graph and ChR, whose objects are ‚Äúmulti-sorted‚Äù sets. The most
obvious forgetful functors, which send a graph to its set of vertices or its set of edges, are
not faithful. However, the functor V ‚äîE : Graph ‚ÜíSet considered in Example 1.3.2(ii)
that sends a graph to the union of its set of vertices and edges is faithful.
Because faithful functors reÔ¨Çect identiÔ¨Åcations between parallel morphisms:
Lemma 1.6.19. If U : C ‚ÜíD is faithful, then any diagram in C whose image commutes in
D also commutes in C.
Proof. Here a ‚Äúdiagram‚Äù in C is a directed graph of morphisms together with a
collection of desired composition relations between composable paths of morphisms. If
f1, . . . , fn and g1, . . . , gm are parallel sequences of composable morphisms in C so that
U fn ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ U f1 = Ugm ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ Ug1
in D, then by faithfulness (and functoriality) of U, fn ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ f1 = gm ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ g1 in C.
‚ñ°
In particular, to prove that a diagram in a concrete category commutes, it suÔ¨Éces to
prove commutativity of the induced diagram of underlying sets. This amounts to showing
that certain composite functions between underlying sets are the same, and this can be
checked by considering the actions of these functions on the elements of their domain.
We close with a word of warning.

need not hold, as illustrated by the following diagram in Ab, in which the outer rectangle

Exercises.
Exercise 1.6.i. Show that any map from a terminal object in a category to an initial one is
an isomorphism. An object that is both initial and terminal is called a zero object.
Exercise 1.6.ii. Show that any two terminal objects in a category are connected by a unique
isomorphism.
Exercise 1.6.iii. Show that any faithful functor reÔ¨Çects monomorphisms.
That is, if
F : C ‚ÜíD is faithful, prove that if F f is a monomorphism in D, then f is a monomorphism
in C. Argue by duality that faithful functors also reÔ¨Çect epimorphisms. Conclude that
in any concrete category, any morphism that deÔ¨Ånes an injection of underlying sets is
a monomorphism and any morphism that deÔ¨Ånes a surjection of underlying sets is an
epimorphism.
Exercise 1.6.iv. Find an example to show that a faithful functor need not preserve epimor-
phisms. Argue by duality, or by another counterexample, that a faithful functor need not
preserve monomorphisms.
Exercise 1.6.v. More speciÔ¨Åcally, Ô¨Ånd a concrete category that contains a monomorphism
whose underlying function is not injective.
Find a concrete category that contains an
epimorphism whose underlying function is not surjective. Exercise 4.5.v explains why the
latter examples may seem less familiar than the former.

1.7. The 2-category of categories
A number of important facts about natural transformations are proven by diagram
chasing. In this section, we deÔ¨Åne ‚Äúvertical‚Äù and ‚Äúhorizontal‚Äù composition operations for
natural transformations. The upshot is that categories, functors, and natural transformations
assemble into a 2-dimensional categorical structure called a 2-category, a deÔ¨Ånition that is
stated at the conclusion.
In French, a natural transformation is called a morphisme de foncteurs. Indeed, for any
Ô¨Åxed pair of categories C and D, there is a functor category DC whose objects are functors
C ‚ÜíD and whose morphisms are natural transformations. Given a functor F : C ‚ÜíD, its
identity natural transformation 1F : F ‚áíF is the natural transformation whose components
(1F)c B 1Fc are identities. The following lemma deÔ¨Ånes the composition of morphisms in
DC.
Lemma 1.7.1 (vertical composition). Suppose Œ±: F ‚áíG and Œ≤: G ‚áíH are natural
transformations between parallel functors F,G, H : C ‚ÜíD.
Then there is a natural
transformation Œ≤ ¬∑ Œ±: F ‚áíH whose components
(Œ≤ ¬∑ Œ±)c B Œ≤c ¬∑ Œ±c
are deÔ¨Åned to be the composites of the components of Œ± and Œ≤.
Proof. Naturality of Œ± and Œ≤ implies that for any f : c ‚Üíc‚Ä≤ in the domain category,
each square, and thus also the composite rectangle, commutes:

Corollary 1.7.2. For any pair of categories C and D, the functors from C to D and natural
transformations between them deÔ¨Åne a category DC.
Proof. It remains only to verify that the composition operation deÔ¨Åned by Lemma
1.7.1 is associative and unital. It suÔ¨Éces to verify these properties componentwise, and
they follow immediately from the associativity and unitality of composition in D.
‚ñ°
Remark 1.7.3 (sizes of functor categories). Care should be taken with size when discussing
functor categories. If C and D are small, then DC is again a small category, but if C and
D are locally small, then DC need not be. This is only guaranteed if D is locally small and
C is small; see Exercise 1.7.i. In summary, the formation of functor categories deÔ¨Ånes a

CHAPTER 2
Universal Properties, Representability, and the Yoneda
Lemma
. . . a mathematical object X is best thought of in
the context of a category surrounding it, and is
determined by the network of relations it enjoys
with all the objects of that category. Moreover, to
understand X it might be more germane to deal
directly with the functor representing it.
Barry Mazur, ‚ÄúThinking about Grothendieck‚Äù
[Maz16]
The aim in this chapter is to explain what it means to say that the natural numbers is the
universal discrete dynamical system, that the Sierpinski space is the universal space with
an open subset, or that the complete graph on n-vertices is the universal n-colored graph.
Universal properties expressed in this plain language manner might appear somewhat ad
hoc, but this is a false impression. By the chapter‚Äôs end, we will see several equivalent ways
in which the notion of universal property can be made precise. The key input to the theory
developed here is something that is a priori non-obvious: a functor valued in the category
of sets. The category of sets plays a special role because traditional approaches to the
foundations of mathematics are based on set theory, with a wide variety of mathematical
objects deÔ¨Åned to be sets with additional structure.
To illustrate, consider the set of vertex colorings of a graph subject to the requirement
that adjacent vertices are assigned distinct colors. There is a contravariant functor from
the category of graphs to the category of sets that takes a graph to the set of n-colorings
of its vertices. To explain the contravariance, note that an n-coloring of a graph G and a
graph homomorphism G‚Ä≤ ‚ÜíG induce an n-coloring of G‚Ä≤ that colors each vertex of G‚Ä≤
to match the color of its image: as graph homomorphisms preserve the incidence relation
on vertices, any two adjacent vertices of G‚Ä≤ are assigned distinct colors. This deÔ¨Ånes the
action of the functor n-Color: Graphop ‚ÜíSet on morphisms.1
The graph with the fewest vertices and fewest edges that can be colored with no less
than n colors is the complete graph on n vertices Kn. Indeed, the functor n-Color encodes
a universal property of the graph Kn in the sense that the graph Kn represents the functor
n-Color. SpeciÔ¨Åcally, this means that there is a natural bijection between the set n-Color(G)
of n-colorings of a graph G and the set of graph homomorphisms G ‚ÜíKn. In ¬ß2.1, we
introduce the notion of a representable functor along with a plethora of examples, intended
to convey the ubiquity of this abstract notion.
1The four color theorem states that if G is a simple planar graph, then the set 4-Color(G) is non-empty.
Functoriality implies that any graph admitting a graph homomorphism to a planar graph also admits a 4-coloring.
For instance, complete bipartite graphs, which are typically non-planar, will admit homomorphisms of this type.
49

50
2. UNIVERSAL PROPERTIES, REPRESENTABILITY, AND THE YONEDA LEMMA
By the Yoneda lemma, which we prove in ¬ß2.2, this universal property uniquely
characterizes the graph Kn. The Yoneda lemma also establishes a correspondence between
natural endomorphisms of the functor n-Color‚Äî‚Äúcolor permutations‚Äù‚Äîand symmetries of
the graph Kn and proves that there is no uniform way to convert an m-coloring into an n-
coloring if m > n, this result deduced from the fact that there are no graph homomorphisms
Km ‚ÜíKn.
The Yoneda lemma is arguably the most important result in category theory, although
it takes some time to explore the depths of the consequences of this simple statement. In
¬ß2.3, we deÔ¨Åne the notion of universal element that witnesses a universal property of some
object in a locally small category. The universal element witnessing the universal property
of the complete graph is an n-coloring of Kn, an element of the set n-Color(Kn). In ¬ß2.4,
we use the Yoneda lemma to show that the pair comprised of an object characterized by
a universal property and its universal element deÔ¨Ånes either an initial or a terminal object
in the category of elements of the functor that it represents. This gives precise meaning
to the term universal: it is a synonym for either ‚Äúinitial‚Äù or ‚Äúterminal,‚Äù with context
disambiguating between the two cases. For instance, Kn is the terminal n-colored graph:
the terminal object in the category of n-colored graphs and graph homomorphisms that
preserve the coloring of vertices.
2.1. Representable functors
The further you go in mathematics, especially
pure mathematics, the more universal properties
you will meet.
Tom Leinster, Basic Category Theory [Lei14]
The most basic formulation of a universal property is to say that a particular object
deÔ¨Ånes an initial or terminal object in its ambient category. The problem with this paradigm
is that the most familiar categories‚Äîfor instance, of sets, spaces, groups, modules, and
so on‚Äîtend to have uninteresting initial and terminal objects. To express the universal
properties of more complicated objects, one has to cook up a less familiar category. For
example:
Example 2.1.1. A set X with an endomorphism f : X ‚ÜíX and a distinguished element x0
is called a discrete dynamical system. This data allows one to consider the discrete-time
evolution of the initial element x0, a sequence deÔ¨Åned by xn+1 B f(xn). The principle of
mathematical recursion asserts that the natural numbers N, the successor function s: N ‚Üí
N, and the element 0 ‚ààN deÔ¨Åne the universal discrete dynamical system: which is to
say, there is a unique function r: N ‚ÜíX so that r(n) = xn for each n, i.e., so that r(0) = x0
and so that the diagram

commutes.
In this section, we introduce a vehicle for expressing universal properties that is less-
contrived than spontaneously generating an appropriate category in which the universal

object is initial or terminal. The notion that we introduce can be understood as a general-

show that it is not a true generalization.
To say that an object c ‚ààC is initial is to say that for all other objects x, the set C(c, x)
is a singleton. Dually, c ‚ààC is terminal, if and only if for all x ‚ààC the set C(x, c) is a
singleton. These properties can be expressed more categorically as characterizations of the
co- and contravariant functors C(c, ‚àí) and C(‚àí, c) represented by c that were introduced in

Definition 2.1.3 (a representable characterization of initial or terminal objects).
(i) An object c in a category C is initial if and only if the functor C(c, ‚àí): C ‚ÜíSet is
naturally isomorphic to the constant functor ‚àó: C ‚ÜíSet that sends every object to
the singleton set.
(ii) An object c ‚ààC is terminal if and only if the functor C(‚àí, c): Cop ‚ÜíSet is naturally
isomorphic to the constant functor ‚àó: Cop ‚ÜíSet that sends every object to the
singleton set.
In terminology we now introduce, DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.3 asserts that C has an initial object if
and only if the constant functor ‚àó: C ‚ÜíSet is representable, and dually, C has a terminal
object if and only if ‚àó: Cop ‚ÜíSet is representable.
Definition 2.1.4.
(i) A covariant or contravariant functor F from a locally small category C to Set is
representable if there is an object c ‚ààC and a natural isomorphism between F and
the functor of appropriate variance2 represented by c, in which case one says that the
functor F is represented by the object c.
(ii) A representation for a covariant functor F is a choice of object c ‚ààC together with
a speciÔ¨Åed natural isomorphism C(c, ‚àí)  F, if F is covariant, or C(‚àí, c)  F, if F
is contravariant.
The domain C of a representable functor is required to be locally small so that the
hom-functors C(c, ‚àí) and C(‚àí, c) are valued in the category of sets.
As in the special case of DeÔ¨Ånition 2.1.3, a representable functor F : C ‚ÜíSet or
F : Cop ‚ÜíSet encodes a universal property of its representing object. Put colloquially,
a universal property of an object X is a description of the covariant functor Hom(X, ‚àí) or
of the contravariant functor Hom(‚àí, X) associated to that object. Many examples occur ‚Äúin
nature.‚Äù3
Example 2.1.5. The following covariant functors are representable.
(i) The identity functor 1Set : Set ‚ÜíSet is represented by the singleton set 1. That is,
for any set X, there is a natural isomorphism Set(1, X)  X that deÔ¨Ånes a bijection

(viii) The forgetful functor U : Top ‚ÜíSet is represented by the singleton space: there is a
natural bijection between elements of a topological space and continuous functions
from the one-point space.
(ix) The functor ob: Cat ‚ÜíSet that takes a small category to its set of objects is
represented by the terminal category 1: a functor 1 ‚ÜíC is no more and no less than
a choice of object in C.
(x) The functor mor: Cat ‚ÜíSet that takes a small category to its set of morphisms is
represented by the category 2: a functor 2 ‚ÜíC is no more and no less than a choice
of morphism in C. In this sense, the category 2 is the free or walking arrow.
(xi) The functor iso: Cat ‚ÜíSet that takes a small category to its set of isomorphisms
(pointing in a speciÔ¨Åed direction) is represented by the category I, with two objects
and exactly one morphism in each hom-set. In this sense, the category I is the free
or walking isomorphism.
(xii) The functor comp: Cat ‚ÜíSet that takes a small category to the set of composable
pairs of morphisms in it is represented by the category 3. Generalizing, the ordinal
n + 1 = 0 ‚Üí1 ‚Üí¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚Üín represents the functor that takes a small category to the
set of paths of n composable morphisms in it.
(xiii) The forgetful functor U : Set‚àó‚ÜíSet is represented by the two-element based set:
based functions out of this set correspond naturally and bijectively to elements of
the target based set, the element in question being the image of the non-basepoint
element.
(xiv) The functor Path: Top ‚ÜíSet that carries a topological space to its set of paths and
the functor Loop: Top‚àó‚ÜíSet that carries a based space to its set of based loops
are each representable by deÔ¨Ånition, by the unit interval I and the based circle S 1,
respectively. A path in X is a continuous function I ‚ÜíX while a (based) loop in X
is a based continuous function S 1 ‚ÜíX.
The adjective ‚Äúfree‚Äù is reserved for universal properties expressed by covariant repre-
sented functors. It could be applied to any of the objects listed in Example 2.1.5: 2 is the
free category with an arrow, S 1 is the free space containing a loop. The dual term ‚Äúcofree,‚Äù
for universal properties expressed by contravariant represented functors, is less commonly
used.

(ii) The functor O: Topop ‚ÜíSet that sends a space to its set of open subsets is represented
by the Sierpinski space S , the topological space with two points, one closed and

This bijection is natural because a

under the function Y ‚ÜíX.

to its set of closed subsets. Composing the natural isomorphisms O  Top(‚àí, S )  C
we see that the closed set and open set functors are naturally isomorphic.
The
composite natural isomorphism carries an open subset to its complement, which is
closed. This recovers the natural isomorphism described in Example 1.4.3(v).
(iv) The functor Hom(‚àí√ó A, B): Setop ‚ÜíSet that sends a set X to the set of functions
X √ó A ‚ÜíB is represented by the set BA of functions from A to B. That is, there is a
natural bijection between functions X √ó A ‚ÜíB and functions X ‚ÜíBA. This natural
isomorphism is referred to as currying in computer science; by Ô¨Åxing a variable in
a two-variable function, one obtains a family of functions in a single variable.
(v) The functor U(‚àí)‚àó: Vectop
k ‚ÜíSet that sends a vector space to the set of vectors in
its dual space is represented by the vector space k, i.e., linear maps V ‚Üík are, by
deÔ¨Ånition, precisely the vectors in the dual space V‚àó.
(vi) For any Ô¨Åxed abelian group A and any n ‚â•0, singular cohomology with coeÔ¨Écients
in A deÔ¨Ånes a functor Hn(‚àí; A): Topop ‚ÜíAb. As in Example 1.3.2(iii), this functor
is a homotopy invariant, factoring through the quotient Top ‚ÜíHtpy to deÔ¨Åne a
functor Hn(‚àí; A): Htpyop ‚ÜíAb. Passing to underlying sets and restricting to a
subcategory of ‚Äúnice‚Äù spaces, such as the CW complexes, the resulting functor
Hn(‚àí; A): Htpyop
CW ‚ÜíSet is represented by the Eilenberg‚ÄìMacLane space K(A, n).
That is, for any CW complex X, homotopy classes of maps X ‚ÜíK(A, n) stand in
bijection with elements of the nth singular cohomology group Hn(X; A) of X with
coeÔ¨Écients in A.
(vii) A classifying space for a topological group G is a CW complex BG that represents
the functor Htpyop
CW ‚ÜíSet that takes a CW complex to the set of isomorphism classes
of principle G-bundles over it.4
Our language asserts that a representation encodes some sort of universal property of
its representing object, but many questions remain:
‚Ä¢ How unique are these universal properties? If two objects represent the same functor,
are they isomorphic?
‚Ä¢ What data is involved in the construction of a natural isomorphism between a repre-
sentable functor F and the functor represented by an object c?
‚Ä¢ How do the universal properties expressed by representable functors relate to initial
and terminal objects‚Äîour Ô¨Årst paradigm for ‚Äúuniversality‚Äù?
Answers to all of these questions will make use of the Yoneda lemma, to which we now
turn.

2.2. The Yoneda lemma
Yoneda enjoyed relating the story of the origins of
this lemma, as follows . . . Yoneda spent a year in
France (apparently in 1954 and 1955). There he
met Saunders Mac Lane. Mac Lane, then visiting
Paris, was anxious to learn from Yoneda, and
commenced an interview with Yoneda in a caf√© at
Gare du Nord. The interview was continued on
Yoneda‚Äôs train until its departure. In its course,
Mac Lane learned about the lemma and
subsequently baptized it.
Saunders Mac Lane, ‚ÄúThe Yoneda lemma‚Äù
[ML98b]
To gain a better understanding of the representations considered in Examples 2.1.5 and
2.1.6 above, it would help to answer the following question: Given a functor F : C ‚ÜíSet,
what data is needed to deÔ¨Åne a natural isomorphism C(c, ‚àí)  F? More generally, what
data is needed to deÔ¨Åne a natural transformation C(c, ‚àí) ‚áíF?
Before addressing the general question, let us cut our teeth on a few warm-up examples.

As a second example, we consider diagrams whose indexing categories are groups.
Example 2.2.2. There is a single object in the category BG whose endomorphisms deÔ¨Åne
a group G.
Thus, there is a unique covariant represented functor BG ‚ÜíSet and a
unique contravariant represented functor BGop ‚ÜíSet. Example 1.3.9 characterized the
functors from BG to Set; in the covariant case, they correspond to left G-sets, and in
the contravariant case, they correspond to right G-sets. Recalling that the elements of G
deÔ¨Åne the set of automorphisms of the unique object in the category BG, we see that the
covariant represented functor is the G-set G, with its action by left multiplication, while the
contravariant represented functor is again G, but with its right action by right multiplication.
Now consider a G-set X : BG ‚ÜíSet.
Example 1.4.3(iv) observed that a natural
transformation œÜ: G ‚áíX is exactly a G-equivariant map œÜ: G ‚ÜíX. Here œÜ: G ‚ÜíX is
the unique component of the natural transformation, and equivariance of this map expresses
the naturality condition. To deÔ¨Åne œÜ we must specify elements œÜ(g) ‚ààX for each g ‚ààG.
Equivariance demands that œÜ(g ¬∑ h) = g ¬∑ œÜ(h). Taking h to be the identity element, we see
that œÜ(g) = g ¬∑ œÜ(e). In other words, the choice of œÜ(e) ‚ààX forces us to deÔ¨Åne œÜ(g) to be
g ¬∑ œÜ(e). Moreover, any choice of œÜ(e) ‚ààX is permitted, because the left action of G on G
is free.5
This argument proves:
Proposition 2.2.3. G-equivariant maps G ‚ÜíX correspond bijectively to elements of X,
identiÔ¨Åed as the image of the identity element e ‚ààG.
For diagrams indexed by the poset œâ and by a group BG, Examples 2.2.1 and 2.2.2
reveal that natural transformations whose domain is a represented functor are determined
by the choice of a single element, which lives in the set deÔ¨Åned by evaluating the codomain
functor at the representing object, and moreover any choice is permitted. In each case,
this element is the image of the identity morphism at the representing object.6 This is no

deÔ¨Ånitions of œÜ(g).

Theorem 2.2.4 (Yoneda lemma). For any functor F : C ‚ÜíSet, whose domain C is locally
small and any object c ‚ààC, there is a bijection
Hom(C(c, ‚àí), F)  Fc
that associates a natural transformation Œ±: C(c, ‚àí) ‚áíF to the element Œ±c(1c) ‚ààFc.
Moreover, this correspondence is natural in both c and F.
As C is locally small but not necessarily small, a priori the collection of natural
transformations Hom(C(c, ‚àí), F) might be large. However, the bijection in the Yoneda
lemma proves that this particular collection of natural transformations indeed forms a set.
The statement of the dual form of Theorem 2.2.4, for contravariant functors, is left to
Exercise 2.2.i. We divide the proof into two parts, Ô¨Årst demonstrating the bijection and then
turning our attention to the naturality statement.

An easy application of the Yoneda lemma completely characterizes natural transfor-

full subcategory of SetCop spanned by the contravariant represented functors, and Cop is
isomorphic to the full subcategory of SetC spanned by the covariant represented functors,
via the canonically-deÔ¨Åned covariant and contravariant Yoneda embeddings.

From the construction of the functors y, it is easy to see that these maps are injections:

Œ±: C(d, ‚àí) ‚áíC(c, ‚àí)

is Œ±d(1d). The natural transformation f ‚àó: C(d, ‚àí) ‚áíC(c, ‚àí) deÔ¨Åned by pre-composition
by f sends 1d to f. Thus, the bijection implies that Œ± = f ‚àó.
‚ñ°
Corollary 2.2.8, that natural transformations between represented functors correspond
to morphisms between the representing objects, is an extraordinarily powerful result, as we
shall gradually discover. Common convention refers to both Theorem 2.2.4 and Corollary
2.2.8 as ‚Äúthe Yoneda lemma.‚Äù
We close this section with two non-categorical applications of the fullness of the
Yoneda embedding. A third even more paradigmatic application appears as Proposition
2.3.9.
Corollary 2.2.9. Every row operation on matrices with n rows is deÔ¨Åned by left multipli-
cation by some n √ó n matrix, namely the matrix obtained by performing the row operation
on the identity matrix.
Proof. Recall MatR is the category whose objects are positive integers and in which a
morphism m ‚Üín is an n√óm matrix, with n rows and m columns, whose entries are elements
of the unital ring R. The elements in the image of the represented functor Hom(‚àí, n) are
matrices with n rows.
The row operations of elementary linear algebra‚Äîfor instance,
replacing the ith row with the sum of the ith and jth row‚ÄîdeÔ¨Åne natural endomorphisms of
Hom(‚àí, n); naturality here follows from linearity of (right) matrix multiplication. Thus, by
Corollary 2.2.8, every row operation must be deÔ¨Ånable by left multiplication by a suitable

2.2. THE YONEDA LEMMA
61
n √ó n matrix. Moreover, Theorem 2.2.4 allows us to identify this matrix: it is obtained by
applying the row operation in question to the n √ó n identity matrix.
‚ñ°
Another corollary of the Yoneda lemma is a classical result known as Cayley‚Äôs the-
orem, stating that any abstract group may be realized as a subgroup of a permutation
group.
Corollary 2.2.10. Any group is isomorphic to a subgroup of a permutation group.
Proof. Regarding a group G as a one-object category BG, Example 2.2.2 identiÔ¨Åes the
image of the covariant Yoneda embedding BG ,‚ÜíSetBGop as the rightG-setG, withG acting
by right multiplication. Corollary 2.2.8 tells us that the only G-equivariant endomorphisms
of the right G-set G are those maps deÔ¨Åned by left multiplication with a Ô¨Åxed element of
G. In particular, any G-equivariant endomorphism of G must be an automorphism, a fact
that is not otherwise obvious.
In this way, the Yoneda embedding deÔ¨Ånes an isomorphism between G and the auto-
morphism group of the right G-set G, an object in SetBGop. Composing with the faithful
forgetful functor SetBGop ‚ÜíSet, we obtain an isomorphism between G and a subgroup of
the automorphism group Sym(G) of the set G.
‚ñ°
Exercises.
Exercise 2.2.i. State and prove the dual to Theorem 2.2.4, characterizing natural transfor-
mations C(‚àí, c) ‚áíF for a contravariant functor F : Cop ‚ÜíSet.
Exercise 2.2.ii. Explain why the Yoneda lemma does not dualize to classify natural trans-
formations from an arbitrary set-valued functor to a represented functor.

2. UNIVERSAL PROPERTIES, REPRESENTABILITY, AND THE YONEDA LEMMA
2.3. Universal properties and universal elements
The Yoneda lemma enables us to answer the questions posed at the end of ¬ß2.1. In
this section, we study the data associated to a representation and prove that a representable
functor deÔ¨Ånes its representing object.
In a locally small category C, any pair of isomorphic objects x  y are representably
isomorphic, meaning that C(‚àí, x)  C(‚àí, y) and C(x, ‚àí)  C(y, ‚àí). This is an immediate
consequence of the functoriality of the Yoneda embeddings C ,‚ÜíSetCop and Cop ,‚ÜíSetC;
see Exercise 2.2.iv. The Yoneda lemma supplies the converse:
Proposition 2.3.1. Consider a pair of objects x and y in a locally small category C.
(i) If either the co- or contravariant functors represented by x and y are naturally
isomorphic, then x and y are isomorphic.
(ii) In particular, if x and y represent the same functor, then x and y are isomorphic.
Proof. The full and faithful Yoneda embeddings C ,‚ÜíSetCop and Cop ,‚ÜíSetC create
isomorphisms (see Exercise 1.5.iv). So an isomorphism between represented functors is
induced by a unique isomorphism between their representing objects, which in particular
must be isomorphic, proving (i). Now (ii) is an immediate consequence: given a functor
represented by both x and y, the representing natural isomorphisms compose to demonstrate
that x and y are representably isomorphic.
‚ñ°
There may be many isomorphisms between the objects x and y appearing in the proof
of Proposition 2.3.1, but there is a unique natural isomorphism commuting with the chosen
representations. On account of this, one typically refers to the representing object of a
representable functor. Category theorists often use the deÔ¨Ånite article ‚Äúthe‚Äù in contexts
where the object in question is well-deÔ¨Åned up to canonical isomorphism. For instance:
Corollary 2.3.2. The full subcategory of C spanned by its terminal objects is either empty
or is a contractible groupoid. In particular, any two terminal objects in C are uniquely
isomorphic.8
A contractible groupoid is a category that is equivalent to the terminal category 1.
Explicitly, a contractible groupoid is a category with a unique morphism in each hom-set.
Proof. By Theorem 2.2.4, natural transformations C(‚àí, t) ‚áíC(‚àí, t‚Ä≤) between the
functors represented by a pair of terminal objects t, t‚Ä≤ ‚ààC stand in bijection with elements
of the singleton set C(t, t‚Ä≤). So there can be at most one morphism between any pair of
terminal objects. Proposition 2.3.1 implies that this morphism is an isomorphism: the
objects t and t‚Ä≤ are terminal if and only if they represent the functor ‚àó: Cop ‚ÜíSet that is
constant at the singleton set.
‚ñ°
The Yoneda lemma also provides a more explicit characterization of the data encoded
by a representation.
Definition 2.3.3. A universal property of an object c ‚ààC is expressed by a representable
functor F together with a universal element x ‚ààFc that deÔ¨Ånes a natural isomorphism
C(c, ‚àí)  F or C(‚àí, c)  F, as appropriate, via the Yoneda lemma.
8Corollary 2.3.2 proves that the so-called ‚Äúuniversal cover‚Äù of a topological space X is not an initial object in the
category of connected covering spaces over X, unless X is simply connected. If it were, the Deck transformation
group, which is isomorphic to the fundamental group œÄ1(X), would necessarily be trivial.

Example 2.3.4. Recall from Example 2.1.5(iv) that the forgetful functor U : Ring ‚ÜíSet is
represented by the ring Z[x]. The universal element, which deÔ¨Ånes the natural isomorphism
(2.3.5)
Ring(Z[x], R)  UR,
is the element x ‚ààZ[x]. As in the proof of the Yoneda lemma, the bijection (2.3.5) is
implemented by evaluating a ring homomorphism œÜ: Z[x] ‚ÜíR at the element x ‚ààZ[x] to
obtain an element œÜ(x) ‚ààR.
Example 2.3.6. A functor E : BG ‚ÜíSet, i.e., a left G-set E, is representable if and only
if there is an isomorphism G  E of left G-sets. This implies that the action of G on E
is free (every stabilizer group is trivial) and transitive (the orbit of any point is the entire
set), and that E is non-empty. Conversely, any non-empty free and transitive left G-set is
representable; a proof is given in Example 2.4.10. By the Yoneda lemma, the data of a
representation for E, i.e., a speciÔ¨Åc isomorphism G  E, is determined by the choice of
an element e ‚ààE, the universal element, serving as the image of the identity e ‚ààG. In
other words, a representable left G-set E is a group that has forgotten its identity element.
A representable G-set is called a G-torsor.
For example, a torsor for the group Rn under addition is n-dimensional aÔ¨Éne space,
commonly denoted by An (or An
R to emphasize the ground Ô¨Åeld). We think of An as being
the collection of points in n-dimensional real space, but without a chosen origin. Thinking
of points in Rn as vectors, the free and transitive action of Rn on An is easy to describe: a
point in An is transported along the vector in question. This action is free, because every
non-zero vector acts on every point non-trivially, and transitive, because any two points in
aÔ¨Éne space can be ‚Äúsubtracted‚Äù to deÔ¨Åne a unique vector in Rn that carries the one to the
other. A choice of an origin point o in An determines coordinates for all of the other points,
hence an isomorphism Rn  An. The point o ‚ààAn is the universal element: the coordinates
for another point p ‚ààAn are deÔ¨Åned to be the unique vector ‚Éóx ‚ààRn so that ‚Éóx ¬∑ o = p.

2. UNIVERSAL PROPERTIES, REPRESENTABILITY, AND THE YONEDA LEMMA
2.4. The category of elements
A universal property for an object c ‚ààC is expressed either by a contravariant functor
F together with a representation C(‚àí, c)  F or by a covariant functor F together with
a representation C(c, ‚àí)  F.
The representations deÔ¨Åne a natural characterization of
the maps into (in the contravariant case) or out of (in the covariant case) the object c.
Proposition 2.3.1 implies that a universal property characterizes the object c ‚ààC up to
isomorphism. More precisely, there is a unique isomorphism between c and any other
object representing F that commutes with the chosen representations.
In such contexts, the phrase ‚Äúc is the universal object in C with an x‚Äù assets that x ‚ààFc
is a universal element in the sense of DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.3, i.e., x is the element of Fc that
classiÔ¨Åes the natural isomorphism that deÔ¨Ånes the representation by the Yoneda lemma. In
this section, we prove that the term ‚Äúuniversal‚Äù is being used in the precise sense alluded
to at the beginning of this chapter: the universal element is either initial or terminal in an
appropriate category. The category in question, called the category of elements, can be
constructed in a canonical way from the data of the representable functor F. The main result
of this section, Proposition 2.4.8, proves that any universal property can be understood as
deÔ¨Åning an initial or terminal object, as variance dictates, completing the promise made in
¬ß2.1.
The category of elements is also used to answer another question inspired by consid-
eration of representable functors: namely, how to determine whether a given set-valued
functor F is representable. And if this is the case, is there a way to identify when an
element x ‚ààFc determines a natural isomorphism C(‚àí, c)  F, rather than simply a natural
transformation C(‚àí, c) ‚áíF?

Example 2.4.3. Recall the functor n-Color: Graphop ‚ÜíSet that carries a graph to the set

pair of n-colored graphs is a graph homomorphism œÜ: G ‚ÜíG‚Ä≤ so that the induced function

coloring of G. In other words, the graph homomorphism œÜ preserves the chosen colorings,
in the sense that each purple vertex of G is carried to a purple vertex of G‚Ä≤. In summary,
R
n-Color is the category of n-colored graphs and color-preserving graph homomorphisms.

Importantly, the category of elements can be used to show that all universal properties
can be encoded by saying that certain data, the universal element of DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.3, deÔ¨Ånes
an initial or terminal object in an appropriate category, namely the category of elements of
the representable functor.
Proposition 2.4.8 (universal elements are universal elements). A covariant set-valued
functor is representable if and only if its category of elements has an initial object. Dually,
a contravariant set-valued functor is representable if and only if its category of elements
has a terminal object.
It is easy to see that this condition is necessary. A natural isomorphism C(c, ‚àí)  F

Proposition 2.4.9. For any functor F : C ‚ÜíSet, the full subcategory of
R
F spanned by

Proof. If F is not representable, then it has no representations and this subcategory

object in
R
F. By Corollary 2.3.2, the subcategory spanned by the initial objects is either
empty or is a contractible groupoid: there exists a unique (iso)morphism between any two
initial objects.

Example 2.4.10. The category of elements of a G-set X : BG ‚ÜíSet is the translation
groupoid TGX introduced in Example 1.5.18: its objects are elements x ‚ààX and its
morphisms g: x ‚Üíy are elements g ‚ààG so that g ¬∑ x = y. By Example 2.3.6, if X is
representable, then any element of X may be chosen as a universal element. By Proposition
2.4.9, it follows that X is representable if and only if
R
X  TGX is a contractible groupoid.
Assuming X is non-empty, this groupoid is contractible if and only if for each pair of
elements x, y ‚ààX, there is a unique g ‚ààG so that g ¬∑ x = y. Existence tells us that the action
of G on X is transitive. Uniqueness, in the case x = y, tells us that it is free. This proves
the claim made in Example 2.3.6 that any free and transitive left G-set is representable.

indexed by a diÔ¨Äerent category C, related by a forgetful functor E ‚ÜíC.13 For instance:

CHAPTER 3
Limits and Colimits
Many properties of mathematical constructions
may be represented by universal properties of
diagrams.
Saunders Mac Lane, Categories for the Working
Mathematician [ML98a]
From a very simple topological space, the real line R with its usual metric, one can build
a wide variety of new topological spaces. Taking products of R with itself, one deÔ¨Ånes
the Euclidean spaces Rn in Ô¨Ånite or inÔ¨Ånite dimensions. The space Rn has interesting
subspaces, deÔ¨Åned to be the solutions to certain continuous polynomial functions Rn ‚ÜíR,
including the (n ‚àí1)-sphere S n‚àí1, which bounds the closed n-disk Dn. From the sphere S n,
one can deÔ¨Åne real projective space RPn as a quotient by identifying each pair of antipodal
points. From spheres and disks one can build tori, the M√∂bius band, the Klein bottle,
and indeed any cell complex through a sequence of gluing constructions, in which disks
are attached to an existing space along their boundary spheres. In each case, the newly
constructed object is a particular set equipped with a speciÔ¨Åc topology. In this chapter, we
will explain how all of these topologies can be deÔ¨Åned in a uniform way, via a universal
property that characterizes the newly constructed space either as a limit or a colimit of a
particular diagram in the category of topological spaces.
Limits and colimits can be deÔ¨Åned in any category. Special cases include constructions
of the inÔ¨Åmum and supremum, free products, cartesian products, direct sums, kernels,
cokernels, Ô¨Åber products, amalgamated free products, inverse limits of sequences, unions,
and even the category of elements, among many others. This chapter begins in ¬ß3.1 by
introducing the abstract notions of limits and colimits and the special terminology used
for particular diagram shapes. We then turn our focus to ‚Äúcomputational‚Äù results, which
describe how more complicated limits and colimits can be built out of simpler ones. To
ground intuition for limits and colimits, these abstract constructions are Ô¨Årst introduced
in ¬ß3.2 in the special case of limits of diagrams valued in the category of sets. Indeed,
as a consequence of the Yoneda lemma, the set-theoretical constructions of limits suÔ¨Éce
to prove general formulae for limits and colimits in any category. To understand this, we
consider a variety of possible interactions between functors and limits in ¬ß3.3, presenting
functors that preserve, reÔ¨Çect, or create limits and colimits. Such functors can be used to
recognize cases in which limits or colimits in one category can be constructed from limits
or colimits in another. This vocabulary is used in ¬ß3.4 to describe the representable nature
of limits and colimits and extend the constructions of general limits from simple limits
from Set to any category.
In ¬ß3.5, we apply the computational theorems from ¬ß3.4 to prove that the categories of
sets, spaces, and categories are each complete and cocomplete, meaning that any diagram
indexed by a small category has both a limit and a colimit.
Most of the ‚Äúalgebraic‚Äù
73

74
3. LIMITS AND COLIMITS
categories introduced in Example 1.1.3 are also complete and cocomplete‚Äîthe category
of Ô¨Åelds being a notable exception‚Äîbut we defer the proof of these facts to ¬ß5.6 where they
can be deduced simultaneously as special cases of a general theorem. In ¬ß3.6, we introduce
limit and colimit functors deÔ¨Åned on categories of diagrams and use the functoriality of the
limit and the colimit to prove the result that motivated Eilenberg and Mac Lane to introduce
the concept of naturality: that naturally isomorphic diagrams have isomorphic limits.
In ¬ß3.7, we explain why few categories admit limits and colimits of diagrams of
unbounded size. In ¬ß3.8, we study the interactions between limits and colimits. While
limits commute with limits and colimits commute with colimits, in general, limits and
colimits fail to commute with each other, with a few notable exceptions that depend both
on the indexing shapes and the ambient category in which the diagrams are valued. To
illustrate, we prove the most famous of these: that Ô¨Ånite limits commute with Ô¨Åltered
colimits in the category of sets.
3.1. Limits and colimits as universal cones
Recall that a diagram of shape J in a category C is a functor F : J ‚ÜíC. Our aim
in this section is to introduce the dual notions of limit and colimit of a diagram, which are
deÔ¨Åned, respectively, to be the universal cones over and under the diagram. The Ô¨Årst task
is to explain what is meant by a cone.
Definition 3.1.1. For any object c ‚ààC and any category J, the constant functor c: J ‚ÜíC
sends every object of J to c and every morphism in J to the identity morphism 1c. The
constant functors deÔ¨Åne an embedding ‚àÜ: C ‚ÜíCJ that sends an object c to the constant
functor at c and a morphism f : c ‚Üíc‚Ä≤ to the constant natural transformation, in which
each component is deÔ¨Åned to be the morphism f.
Definition 3.1.2. A cone over a diagram F : J ‚ÜíC with summit or apex c ‚ààC is
a natural transformation Œª: c ‚áíF whose domain is the constant functor at c.

The limit of F is most simply described as the universal cone over the diagram F,
while the colimit is the universal cone under F. As discussed in Chapter 2, there are two
equivalent ways to understand this universal property. A limit may be deÔ¨Åned to be a
representation for a particular contravariant functor or as a terminal object in its category
of elements; dually, a colimit is a representation for a particular covariant functor and,
equivalently, is an initial object in its category of elements. As is always the case for
representable deÔ¨Ånitions, limits and colimits can be contemplated for any diagram valued
in any category, but in any particular case these universal objects may or may not exist.
Definition 3.1.5 (limits and colimits I). For any diagram F : J ‚ÜíC, there is a functor

the Yoneda lemma, a limit consists of an object lim F ‚ààC together with a universal cone
Œª: lim F ‚áíF, called the limit cone, deÔ¨Åning the natural isomorphism
C(‚àí, lim F)  Cone(‚àí, F) .

that sends c to the set of cones under F with nadir c. A colimit of F is a representation for
Cone(F, ‚àí). By the Yoneda lemma, a colimit consists of an object colim F ‚ààC together
with a universal cone Œª: F ‚áícolim F, called the colimit cone, deÔ¨Åning the natural
isomorphism
C(colim F, ‚àí)  Cone(F, ‚àí) .
Applying Proposition 2.4.8, limits and colimits may also be deÔ¨Åned to be terminal and
initial objects, respectively, in the appropriate categories of elements.

Limits and colimits of a diagram F of shape J might be denoted by limJ F or colimJ F
for emphasis, particularly when multiple diagram shapes are involved. On other occasions,
no special notation is used: to say that a cone is a ‚Äúlimit cone‚Äù is to assert that its summit
is a limit, however it is denoted.
On account of the following proposition, it is common to use the deÔ¨Ånite article ‚Äúthe‚Äù
to refer to a limit or a colimit of a Ô¨Åxed diagram because these objects are well-deÔ¨Åned up
to a unique isomorphism:
Proposition 3.1.7 (essential uniqueness of limits and colimits). Given any two limit cones
Œª: ‚Ñì‚áíF and Œª‚Ä≤ : ‚Ñì‚Ä≤ ‚áíF over a common diagram F, there is a unique isomorphism ‚Ñì ‚Ñì‚Ä≤
that commutes with the legs of the limit cones.
Note that this result does not prohibit limit objects from having non-trivial automor-
phisms. If Œª: ‚Ñì‚áíF is a limit cone and œÉ: ‚Ñì ‚Ñìis any automorphism of ‚Ñì, then ŒªœÉ: ‚Ñì‚áíF
is another limit cone. However, Proposition 3.1.7 implies that the only automorphism of ‚Ñì
that commutes with the speciÔ¨Åed limit cone Œª is the identity.
Proof. The limit cones Œª: ‚Ñì‚áíF and Œª‚Ä≤ : ‚Ñì‚Ä≤ ‚áíF each deÔ¨Åne terminal objects in
the category of cones over F. By Corollary 2.3.2, the terminal objects in any category
deÔ¨Åne a contractible groupoid. This means, in particular, that these objects are uniquely
isomorphic in the category of cones, i.e., that there is a unique isomorphism between ‚Ñìand
‚Ñì‚Ä≤ commuting with the cone legs.

Remark 3.1.8 (limit and colimit diagrams). The data of a diagram, together with a limit
cone over it, is called a limit diagram and the data of a diagram, together with a colimit
cone under it, is called a colimit diagram. A J-indexed diagram and a cone over it‚Äîsuch
as displayed in (3.1.4)‚Äîcombine to deÔ¨Åne a diagram indexed by the category J‚óÅ, which has

diagram and a cone under it combine to deÔ¨Åne a diagram indexed by the category J‚ñ∑, which

3. LIMITS AND COLIMITS
Definition 3.1.11. A terminal object is often regarded as a trivial special case of a
product, where the indexing category is empty. A cone over an empty diagram is just an
object in the target category, its summit, together with no additional data. Any morphism
between the summits deÔ¨Ånes a map of empty cones. Thus, the category of cones in this
special case is just the ambient category itself and so the limit, a terminal object in this
category, is just a terminal object in the sense of DeÔ¨Ånition 1.6.14.
Example 3.1.12. The category 1 with one object and only its identity morphism is the
terminal category, the terminal object in Cat or in CAT.
Definition 3.1.13. An equalizer is a limit of a diagram indexed by the parallel pair, the
category ‚Ä¢ ‚áí‚Ä¢ with two objects and two parallel non-identity morphisms. A diagram of
this shape is simply a parallel pair of morphisms f, g: A ‚áíB in the target category C.
A cone over this diagram with summit C consists of a pair of morphisms a: C ‚ÜíA and
b: C ‚ÜíB so that fa = b and ga = b; these two equations correspond to the naturality
conditions (3.1.3) imposed by each of the two non-identity morphisms in the indexing
category. Together, they assert that fa = ga; the morphism b is necessarily equal to this
common composite. Thus, a cone over a parallel pair f, g: A ‚áíB is represented by a
single morphism a: C ‚ÜíA so that fa = ga.
The equalizer h: E ‚ÜíA is the universal arrow with this property. The limit diagram

The forms of the universal properties that characterize limits and colimits are quite
useful when one is asked to deÔ¨Åne a map into a limit object or out of a colimit object.
Moreover, the uniqueness statements in these universal properties can be used to prove
the commutativity of a diagram whose initial vertex is a colimit object or whose terminal
vertex is a limit object. We illustrate this in the simplest case, for products and coproducts,
but these remarks apply more generally.

The examples mentioned above serve to illustrate the pervasiveness of limits and
colimits among interesting mathematical objects, but how does one prove that a particular
diagram has a limit or colimit and construct one if it exists? There are two general strategies.
The Ô¨Årst is to guess and check. A conjectured limit or colimit cone, such as displayed in
(3.1.20), can often be shown to have the appropriate universal property via an elementary
argument.7
A second strategy is to construct a limit of a more complicated diagram out of limits
of simpler diagrams, whose constructions are easier to understand. In the next section, we
begin to explore this viewpoint in the category of sets.
Exercises.
Exercise 3.1.i. For a Ô¨Åxed diagram F ‚ààCJ, describe the actions of the cone functors
Cone(‚àí, F): Cop ‚ÜíSet and Cone(F, ‚àí): C ‚ÜíSet on morphisms in C.

naturally isomorphic to Hom(‚àÜ(‚àí), F), the restriction of the hom functor for the category
CJ along the constant functor embedding deÔ¨Åned in 3.1.1.
Exercise 3.1.iii. Prove that the category of cones over F ‚ààCJ is isomorphic to the comma
category ‚àÜ‚ÜìF formed from the constant functor ‚àÜ: C ‚ÜíCJ and the functor F : 1 ‚ÜíCJ.
Argue by duality that the category of cones under F is the comma category F ‚Üì‚àÜ.
Exercise 3.1.iv. Give a second proof of Proposition 3.1.7 by using the universal properties

3.2. Limits in the category of sets
While a limit can be deÔ¨Åned for any diagram of any shape valued in any category,
the ability to state the deÔ¨Ånition is no guarantee that an object with that universal property
exists. In this section, we restrict consideration to diagrams valued in the category of sets
and give a constructive proof that a limit exists for any set-valued diagram that satisÔ¨Åes
an important size constraint. Special cases of this general construction give computations
of products, equalizers, pullbacks, and inverse limits of sequences of sets, the formulae
for which provide intuition for general results that facilitate the computation of limits and
colimits in more exotic categories.

3.2. LIMITS IN THE CATEGORY OF SETS
85
Definition 3.2.1. A diagram is small if its indexing category is a small category.
A
category C is complete if it admits limits of all small diagrams valued in C and is cocomplete
if it admits colimits of all small diagrams valued in C.

3. LIMITS AND COLIMITS
3.3. Preservation, reÔ¨Çection, and creation of limits and colimits
Theorem 3.2.13 would be of somewhat limited interest if it only provided formulae for
limits of diagrams of sets. In ¬ß3.4, we prove the analogous formulae for limits or colimits
of diagrams valued in any category by reducing the proofs of these general results to the
case of limits in Set. This strategy succeeds because limits and colimits in a general locally
small category are deÔ¨Åned representably in terms of limits in the category of sets.
The precise meaning of the slogan ‚Äúlimits and colimits are deÔ¨Åned representably using
limits of sets‚Äù is explained in the next section. First, we introduce language to describe a
variety of possible ways in which a functor can mediate between the limits and colimits in
its domain and codomain categories.
Definition 3.3.1. For any class of diagrams K : J ‚ÜíC valued in C, a functor F : C ‚ÜíD
‚Ä¢ preserves those limits if for any diagram K : J ‚ÜíC and limit cone over K, the image
of this cone deÔ¨Ånes a limit cone over the composite diagram FK : J ‚ÜíD;
‚Ä¢ reÔ¨Çects those limits if any cone over a diagram K : J ‚ÜíC, whose image upon applying
F is a limit cone for the diagram FK : J ‚ÜíD, is a limit cone over K; and
‚Ä¢ creates those limits if whenever FK : J ‚ÜíD has a limit in D, there is some limit cone
over FK that can be lifted to a limit cone over K, and moreover F reÔ¨Çects the limits in
the class of diagrams.
DeÔ¨Ånition 3.3.1 dualizes to describe what it means for a functor to preserve, reÔ¨Çect,
or create a class of colimits. The terms ‚Äúpreserves,‚Äù ‚ÄúreÔ¨Çects,‚Äù and ‚Äúcreates‚Äù are used
more generally. For instance, Exercise 1.5.iv shows that a fully faithful functor preserves,
reÔ¨Çects, and creates isomorphisms.
Remark 3.3.2. To ground intuition for these terms, the following metaphor may prove
helpful: a functor F : C ‚ÜíD maps from ‚Äúupstairs‚Äù (the category C) to ‚Äúdownstairs‚Äù (the
category D). An upstairs diagram (a functor K : J ‚ÜíC) sits above a downstairs diagram
(by composing with F).
Now F preserves limits if any upstairs limit cone maps to a downstairs limit cone. F
reÔ¨Çects limits if any upstairs cone that maps to a downstairs limit cone is also an upstairs
limit cone. Finally, F creates limits if the mere presence of a downstairs limit cone implies
the existence of an upstairs limit cone, and if any cone sitting above a limit cone is a limit
cone. The following diagram illustrates this transfer of information in the case of a pullback
diagram c‚Ä≤ ‚Üíc ‚Üêc‚Ä≤‚Ä≤ in C:
C

The context in which it is of greatest interest to have a functor F : C ‚ÜíD that creates
limits or colimits is when the codomain category D is already known to have the (co)limits
in question. In such cases, a functor that creates (co)limits both preserves and reÔ¨Çects them:

3.3. PRESERVATION, REFLECTION, AND CREATION OF LIMITS AND COLIMITS

Proposition 3.3.3. If F : C ‚ÜíD creates limits for a particular class of diagrams in C and
D has limits of those diagrams, then C admits those limits and F preserves them.
Proof. For any diagram K : J ‚ÜíC in the class, the hypothesis asserts that there is a
limit cone ¬µ: d ‚áíFK in D. As F creates these limits, there must be a limit cone Œª: c ‚áíK
in C whose image under F is isomorphic to ¬µ (because any two limit cones over a common
diagram are isomorphic; see Exercise 3.1.iv). This shows that C admits this class of limits.
To see that F preserves them, consider another limit cone Œª‚Ä≤ : c‚Ä≤ ‚áíK. By Proposition
3.1.7, the two limit cones in C are isomorphic and by composing isomorphisms we see that
the cone FŒª‚Ä≤ : Fc‚Ä≤ ‚áíFK is isomorphic to the limit cone ¬µ: d ‚áíFK. This implies that
FŒª‚Ä≤ : Fc‚Ä≤ ‚áíFK is again a limit cone, proving that F preserves these limits.
‚ñ°
Important families of limit-preserving functors appear in the next section and again
in the next chapter. For now, let us remark that limit-preservation can be an important
hypothesis:
Definition 3.3.4. Let X be a topological space and write O(X) for the poset of open subsets,

Ui ‚à©U j has colimit U.8 A presheaf F : O(X)op ‚ÜíSet is a sheaf if it preserves these
colimits, sending them to limits in Set. Applying Theorem 3.2.13, the hypothesis is that
for any open cover {Ui}i‚ààI of U, the following is an equalizer diagram:

The following lemmas introduce general classes of examples of functors that reÔ¨Çect or

Lemma 3.3.5. Any full and faithful functor reÔ¨Çects any limits and colimits that are present
in its codomain.
Proof. Exercise 3.3.ii.
‚ñ°
Lemma 3.3.6. Any equivalence of categories preserves, reÔ¨Çects, and creates any limits and
colimits that are present in its domain or codomain.
Proof. Exercise 3.3.iii.
‚ñ°
With the exception of the equivalences, the functors that one meets in practice that
create certain limits and colimits tend to do so strictly, in the sense of the following
deÔ¨Ånition.
Definition 3.3.7. A functor F : C ‚ÜíD strictly creates limits for a given class of diagrams
if for any diagram K : J ‚ÜíC in the class and limit cone over FK : J ‚ÜíD,
‚Ä¢ there exists a unique lift of that cone to a cone over K, and
‚Ä¢ moreover, this lift deÔ¨Ånes a limit cone in C.
A category J is connected if it is non-empty and if, in its underlying directed graph,
every pair of objects is connected by a Ô¨Ånite zig-zag of morphisms. A diagram is connected
if it is indexed by a connected category.

A Ô¨Ånal result shows that functor categories inherit limits and colimits, deÔ¨Åned ‚Äúobject-

is the limit of the J-indexed diagram in C whose objects are the objects F j(a) ‚ààC. This
result is analogous to familiar operations on functions: e.g., the sum of a Ô¨Ånite collection
of abelian group homomorphisms f j : A ‚ÜíC is the homomorphism deÔ¨Åned at a ‚ààA by
forming the sum P
j f j(a) in the abelian group C.
To state this result precisely, we consider the objects of a small indexing category A as
a maximal discrete subcategory ob A ,‚ÜíA.

Proposition 3.3.9. If A is small, then the forgetful functor CA ‚ÜíCob A strictly creates all
limits and colimits that exist in C. These limits are deÔ¨Åned objectwise, meaning that for
each a ‚ààA, the evaluation functor eva : CA ‚ÜíC preserves all limits and colimits existing
in C.
Proof. The functor category Cob A is isomorphic to the ob A-indexed product of the
category C with itself, deÔ¨Åned in 1.3.12; as the name would suggest, categorical products
deÔ¨Åne products in CAT. By the universal property of products, a diagram of shape J in a
product category Q
ob A C is an ob A-indexed family of diagrams J ‚ÜíC. It is easy to see
that a limit of each of these component diagrams assembles into a limit for the diagram in
Cob A  Q
ob A C. In particular, Cob A has all limits or colimits that C does, and these are
preserved by the evaluation functors eva : Cob A ‚ÜíC.
To show that CA ‚ÜíCob A strictly creates all limits and colimits, we must show that for
any F : J ‚ÜíCA the ob A-indexed family of objects lim j‚ààJ F j(a) extends to a functor on A
valued in C. The universal property of the limit construction is used to deÔ¨Åne the action
of this functor on morphisms in A. The uniqueness statement in this universal property
then implies that this construction is functorial. The remaining details are left as Exercise
3.3.vi.

Remark 3.4.15 (on generalized elements). A direct proof of Theorem 3.4.12 simply con-
structs the diagram (3.4.13) in C and checks that the coequalizer of c and d has the universal
property that deÔ¨Ånes colimJ F. There is a sense in which this direct proof is quite similar to
our strategy‚Äîto Ô¨Årst verify the result in Set and then apply representability‚Äîby something
called the philosophy of generalized elements, Ô¨Årst articulated by Lawvere [Law05].
Elements of a set A stand in bijection with morphisms a: 1 ‚ÜíA in the category of
sets. By composition, morphisms f : A ‚ÜíB in the category of sets act on elements: the
composite fa encodes the element f(a).10 In any category C, a generalized element of
an object A is a morphism a: X ‚ÜíA with codomain A. Morphisms f : A ‚ÜíB in C act
on X-shaped elements by composition: the composite fa encodes an X-shaped generalized
element of B. In this terminology, the contravariant represented functor
Hom(‚àí, A): Cop ‚ÜíSet
sends an object X to the set of X-shaped generalized elements of A. Since the Yoneda
embedding C ,‚ÜíSetCop is fully faithful, no information about A is lost by considering
instead the functor Hom(‚àí, A) of generalized elements of A.
In particular, to show that a parallel pair of morphisms f, g: A ‚áíB are equal, it suÔ¨Éces
to show that f and g induce the same functions on generalized elements. Or to show that
a cone with summit A is a limit cone, it suÔ¨Éces to show, for generalized elements of any
shape, that the induced functions deÔ¨Åne a limit cone in Set: this argument establishes the
representable universal property of the limit. See [Lei08] for an application of this principle
of ‚Äúdoing without diagrams‚Äù in more sophisticated contexts.
We conclude this section with an informal illustration of the philosophy of generalized
elements by showing that Ô¨Ånite products and equalizers can be constructed out of a terminal
object 1 and (iterated) pullbacks.

3.5. Complete and cocomplete categories
Theorems, such as 3.4.12 and 3.4.18, that reduce the construction of general limits and
colimits to a handful of simple ones are particularly useful when it comes to proving that
a category is complete or cocomplete‚Äîthat is, has all small limits or colimits‚Äîor that
a functor is continuous or cocontinuous‚Äîthat is, preserves all small limits or colimits.

100
3. LIMITS AND COLIMITS
The proofs reduce to checking a few cases. In this section, we describe the constructions
of limits and colimits in various categories, in most cases leaving the proofs that these
constructions have the claimed universal properties to the reader.
Many of the concrete categories of our acquaintance are complete and cocomplete
including Set, Top, Set‚àó, Top‚àó, Cat or CAT, Group, Ab, Ring, ModR, among others. Here,
we restrict consideration to the Ô¨Årst six cases. A result from categorical universal algebra
specializes to prove that the latter categories are complete and cocomplete (see Theorem
5.6.12).
In the Ô¨Årst example, half the work is done already: Theorem 3.2.6 demonstrates that
Set is complete.
Proposition 3.5.1. The category Set is cocomplete.
Proof. By Theorem 3.4.12, it suÔ¨Éces to show that Set has coproducts and coequal-
izers. The former are given by disjoint unions, while the coequalizer of a parallel pair of
functions f, g: A ‚áíB is the quotient of the set B by the equivalence relation generated by
the relations f(a) ‚àºg(a) for each a ‚ààA.
‚ñ°
Proposition 3.5.2. The category Top of spaces is complete and cocomplete.
Proof. The underlying set functor U : Top ‚ÜíSet is represented by the singleton
space ‚àó. Hence, Proposition 3.4.5 implies that if Top has any limits, they must be formed
by topologizing the limit of the diagram of underlying sets, as was argued in the case of the
cartesian product in Example 3.1.10.
As the name suggests, the product of a family of spaces XŒ± is formed by giving
the cartesian product Q
Œ± XŒ± the product topology, which is the coarsest topology so that
each of the projection functions œÄŒ≤ : Q
Œ± XŒ± ‚ÜíXŒ≤ is continuous.
The equalizer of a
pair of continuous functions f, g: X ‚áíY is given by assigning the equalizer E ‚äÇX of
the underlying functions the subspace topology, which also may be characterized as the
coarsest topology on E so that the inclusion E ,‚ÜíX is continuous.
Dually, we might guess that colimits in Top are formed by topologizing the colimit of
the diagram of underlying sets; Theorem 4.5.3 implies that the colimit must be constructed
in this manner. The coproduct is formed by assigning the disjoint union `

topology so that each of the coproduct-inclusions ŒπŒ≤ : XŒ≤ ‚Üí`

Œ± XŒ±. The coequalizer of
a pair of continuous functions f, g: X ‚áíY is constructed by Ô¨Årst forming the set-theoretic
quotient Y/‚àºof Y under the equivalence relation generated by the relations f(x) ‚àºg(x) for
each point x ‚ààX. The set Y/‚àºis then given the quotient topology, under which a subset in
Y/‚àºis open if and only if its preimage in Y is open; this is the Ô¨Ånest topology so that the
quotient map Y ‚Ü†Y/‚àºis continuous.
Theorem 3.4.12 and its dual now imply that Top has all small limits and colimits. The
constructions described here demonstrate moreover that the forgetful functor U : Top ‚ÜíSet
preserves them.
‚ñ°
Example 3.5.3. Any limit or colimit in Top can be constructed in the way prescribed in
the proof of Proposition 3.5.2: by forming the limit or colimit of the diagram of underlying
sets and then topologizing the resulting space with the coarsest (in the case of limits) or
Ô¨Ånest (in the case of colimits) topologies so that the legs of the limit or colimit cones are
continuous.

Completeness and cocompleteness of Set and of Top implies the same for Set‚àóand
Top‚àóas a special case11 of a general result:
Proposition 3.5.5. If C is complete and cocomplete, then so are the slice categories c/C
and C/c for any c ‚ààC.
Proof. The statements are dual, so it suÔ¨Éces to prove the result for c/C. By Proposition
3.3.8, if C is complete and cocomplete, then c/C has all limits and all connected colimits,
created by the forgetful functor Œ†: c/C ‚ÜíC.
Thus, c/C is complete and to prove
cocompleteness it remains only to show that c/C has coproducts. By the construction of
Lemma 3.4.16, generalized to higher arity coproducts, it suÔ¨Éces to show that c/C has an
initial object, which it does: the identity 1c is initial.
Note that this proof also tells us how the coproduct is constructed: the coproduct of a
family of objects c ‚ÜíxŒ± is the map from c to the colimit of the diagram deÔ¨Åned by these
morphisms in C (an ‚Äúasterisk‚Äù with source c and all the morphisms pointing out).12
‚ñ°
Next we consider limits of diagrams of categories. Examples 2.1.5(ix) and (x) reveal
that the functors ob, mor: Cat ‚áíSet are represented by the ordinal categories 1 and 2. By
Theorem 3.4.6(i), both functors preserve limits, which means that if the limit of a diagram
of categories exists, then its set of objects must be the limit of the underlying diagrams
of object-sets and its set of morphisms must be the limit of the underlying diagrams of
morphism-sets. Furthermore, domains, codomains, and identities are also preserved, as
each of these maps is expressible, by Exercise 2.1.i, as a natural transformation between
representable functors. A similar argument involving the category 3 proves that composition
is preserved as well. These observations suggest candidates for the product of any categories
and for the equalizer of any parallel pair of functors, allowing us to prove that:
Proposition 3.5.6. The categories Cat and CAT are complete.

3.5. COMPLETE AND COCOMPLETE CATEGORIES
103
The proof of cocompleteness of Cat is deferred to Corollary 4.5.16, where it is deduced
as a special case of a general result that gives an explicit construction of the colimit of any
diagram of categories. The rough idea is to embed the category of categories into a larger
category in which colimits are more easily understood and then ‚ÄúreÔ¨Çect‚Äù the colimits from
the larger category back into Cat.
Before considering a Ô¨Ånal class of examples, note that completeness and cocomplete-
ness of a category are equivalence-invariant. In particular, a preorder is complete and
cocomplete if and only if its skeleton, a poset, is so.
Proposition 3.5.9. A poset P is complete and cocomplete as a category if and only if it is
a complete lattice, that is, if and only if every subset A ‚äÇP has both an inÔ¨Åmum (greatest
lower bound) and a supremum (least upper bound).
Proof. In any poset (or preorder), a limit of a diagram is an inÔ¨Åmum of its objects,
while a colimit of a diagram is a supremum of its objects. Whether or not there are any
morphisms in the diagram makes no diÔ¨Äerence because all diagrams in a preorder commute.
In particular, any collection of morphisms with common domain deÔ¨Ånes a cone over any
diagram whose objects are indexed by the codomains.
‚ñ°
With this work behind us, it is reasonable to ask: What are all these limits and colimits
good for? One use of a handful of simple limit constructions is to deÔ¨Åne a notion of
equivalence relation internally to a general category. Other signiÔ¨Åcant applications will
appear in ¬ß4.6, where limits and colimits are used to deÔ¨Åne functors.

3.6. Functoriality of limits and colimits
Proposition 3.1.7 demonstrates that limits and colimits are each well-deÔ¨Åned up to
unique isomorphism when they exist. In this section, we establish the functoriality of the
construction of limits or colimits of diagrams of a Ô¨Åxed shape, and explore its consequences.
An important point is that functoriality of the limit is not the same as canonicity. In most
cases, there are many isomorphic objects that deÔ¨Åne the limit, and there is seldom a
reason to prefer any one to the others. To illustrate this point, we present an example that
demonstrates that even when limit objects exist uniquely, as is the case for instance in a
skeletal category, it does not necessarily follow that canonical isomorphisms induced by
the universal properties of these objects are identities.
Proposition 3.6.1. If C has all J-shaped limits, then a choice of a limit for each diagram
deÔ¨Ånes the action on objects of a functor limJ : CJ ‚ÜíC.
Note that this functor is not canonically deÔ¨Åned but rather requires an arbitrary choice
of a limit to serve as ‚Äúthe‚Äù limit for each diagram.
Proof. Choose a limit and a limit cone for each diagram F ‚ààCJ. This deÔ¨Ånes the
action of limJ : CJ ‚ÜíC on objects. It remains only to deÔ¨Åne the action of limJ : CJ ‚ÜíC
on morphisms. The vertical composite of a natural transformation Œ±: F ‚áíG and the limit
cone Œª: limJ F ‚áíF for F deÔ¨Ånes a cone
limJ F

limJ Œ±: limJ F ‚ÜílimJ G. To illustrate, a morphism in C‚Ä¢‚Üí‚Ä¢‚Üê‚Ä¢ is a pair of solid-arrow

More generally, the construction of Proposition 3.6.1 implies that a natural transfor-
mation between diagrams gives rise to a morphism between their limits or between their
colimits, whenever these exist. Whether the codomain category has all limits or colimits
of that shape is irrelevant. Moreover, whenever the natural transformation is a natural
isomorphism, the induced map between the limits or colimits is an isomorphism.
Corollary 3.6.3. A natural isomorphism between diagrams induces a naturally-deÔ¨Åned
isomorphism between their limits or colimits, whenever these exist.
Proof. The proof of Proposition 3.6.1 deÔ¨Ånes a limit functor from the full subcategory
of CJ spanned by those functors admitting limits in C. This functor, like all functors,
preserves isomorphisms (Lemma 1.3.8).
‚ñ°
The main computational motivation for Eilenberg and Mac Lane‚Äôs formulation of the
concept of naturality was to be able to carefully and correctly formulate this result. While
the general theory of limits and colimits had not yet been developed, [EM45, 21.2] proves
Corollary 3.6.3 in the special case of directed diagrams. Indeed, naturality is a necessary
condition in Corollary 3.6.3:
Example 3.6.4. For any group G, a pair of G-sets X, Y : BG ‚áíSet are objectwise isomor-
phic just when their underlying sets are isomorphic. The functors X and Y are naturally
isomorphic just when there exists an equivariant isomorphism, that is, a bijection X
‚àí‚ÜíY

108
3. LIMITS AND COLIMITS
that commutes with the actions by each g ‚ààG. This second condition is stronger than the
Ô¨Årst.
For instance, consider the group Z/2 and the Z/2-sets 2, the two-element set with a
trivial Z/2-action, and 2‚Ä≤, the two-element set where the generator of Z/2 acts by exchanging
the two elements. We may regard 2 and 2‚Ä≤ as functors BZ/2 ‚ÜíSet from which perspective
they are objectwise isomorphic (because both sets have two elements) but not naturally
isomorphic (because there is no isomorphism that commutes with the Z/2-actions).
The limit of a functor X : BG ‚ÜíSet is the set of G-Ô¨Åxed points (see Example 3.2.12)
and the colimit is the set of G-orbits (by Exercise 3.5.i). In the case of our pair of Z/2-sets,
lim 2 has two elements while lim 2‚Ä≤ is empty, and colim 2 has two elements while colim 2‚Ä≤
is a singleton.
Example 3.6.5. To a Ô¨Ånite set X, we can associate the set Sym(X) of its permutations or
the set Ord(X) of its total orderings. Both sets have |X|! elements. These constructions are
functorial, not with respect to all maps of Ô¨Ånite sets, but with respect to the bijections: the
functor Sym: Finiso ‚ÜíFin acts by conjugation and the functor Ord: Finiso ‚ÜíFin acts by
translation. However, the objectwise isomorphism Sym(X)  Ord(X) is not natural, and
indeed limits or colimits of restrictions of these diagrams need not be isomorphic.
Combinatorialists refer to a functor F : Finiso ‚ÜíFin as a species.16 The image F(n)
of the n-element set n is the set of labeled F-structures on n. The set of unlabeled
F-structures on n is deÔ¨Åned by restricting Finiso to the full subcategory spanned by the
n-element set, i.e., to the symmetric group Œ£n regarded as the group of automorphisms of
the object n ‚ààFiniso, and forming the colimit of the diagram
BŒ£n ,‚ÜíFiniso
F‚àí‚ÜíFin .
Because Ord and Sym are objectwise isomorphic, the sets of labeled Sym-structures and
labeled Ord-structures are isomorphic. However, the set of unlabeled Sym-structures on n
is the set of conjugacy classes of permutations of n-elements, while the set of unlabeled
Ord-structures on n is trivial: all linear orders on n are isomorphic. See [Joy81] for more.
A discussion of the functoriality of chosen limits and colimits should come with a few
warnings. Limits, when they exist, are unique up to a unique isomorphism commuting with
the maps in the limit cone. But this is not the same thing as saying that limits are unique
on the nose: even if there is a unique object satisfying the universal property of the limit,
each of its automorphisms gives rise to a distinct limit cone. Moreover, choices of limits
of diagrams of Ô¨Åxed shape, as required to deÔ¨Åne the limit functor of Proposition 3.6.1,
can seldom be made compatibly. For instance, Freyd has shown that in any category with
pullbacks, it is possible to choose canonical pullbacks so that the ‚Äúhorizontal‚Äù composite
of canonical pullback squares, in the sense of Exercise 3.1.viii, is the canonical pullback
of the composite rectangle; however, it is not possible to also arrange so that the ‚Äúvertical‚Äù
composites of canonical pullback squares are canonical pullbacks [FS90, ¬ß1.4].
Even when the chosen limit objects are equal, the various natural isomorphisms asso-
ciated to the limit constructions might not be identities. To illustrate this, Ô¨Årst observe:
Lemma 3.6.6. For any triple of objects X, Y, Z in a category with binary products, there is
a unique natural isomorphism X √ó (Y √ó Z)  (X √ó Y) √ó Z commuting with the projections
to X, Y, and Z.
Proof. Exercise 3.6.ii.
