3.7. Size matters
Recall a category is complete if it admits a limit for any diagram whose domain is
a small category. In this section, we explain the reason for this size restriction. As a
warm-up, the following lemma highlights a commonly existing large limit. Somewhat
peculiarly, an initial object, which is deÔ¨Åned to be the colimit of the empty diagram, may
also be characterized as the limit of the identity functor:
Lemma 3.7.1. For any category C, the identity functor 1C : C ‚ÜíC admits a limit if and
only if C has an initial object.
Proof. Consider a limit cone (Œªc : ‚Ñì‚Üíc)c‚ààC. The legs of the limit cone demonstrate
that ‚Ñìis a weakly initial object, admitting at least one morphism to every object c ‚ààC. To
show that Œªc is the only morphism from ‚Ñìto c, observe that for any morphism f : ‚Ñì‚Üíc,

110
3. LIMITS AND COLIMITS
commutativity of the limit cone implies that fŒª‚Ñì= Œªc. So to prove that ‚Ñìis initial, it suÔ¨Éces
to show that the cone leg Œª‚Ñì: ‚Ñì‚Üí‚Ñìis the identity.
Considering Œªc as a morphism in the diagram 1C, the cone condition tells us that
ŒªcŒª‚Ñì= Œªc. From this equation, we see that Œª‚ÑìdeÔ¨Ånes a factorization of the cone Œª: ‚Ñì‚áí1C
through itself. By uniqueness of such factorizations, we conclude that Œª‚Ñì= 1‚Ñì, as desired.
The veriÔ¨Åcation that an initial object deÔ¨Ånes a limit of 1C is left as Exercise 3.7.i.
‚ñ°
By the size restrictions inherent in the notion of completeness, a complete (large)
category need not have an initial object. As it turns out, complete small categories are hard
to come by. To explain this, we require a precise deÔ¨Ånition of the cardinality of a small
category.
Definition 3.7.2. The cardinality of a small category is the cardinality of the set of its
morphisms. A category whose cardinality is less than Œ∫ is called Œ∫-small.
A Œ∫-small diagram is one whose indexing category is Œ∫-small. The following result
explains why categories tend only to have limits or colimits of diagrams of smaller size.
Proposition 3.7.3 (Freyd). Any Œ∫-small category that admits all Œ∫-small limits or all
Œ∫-small colimits is a preorder.
This result remains true under less restrictive completeness hypotheses, which the
reader will have no trouble formulating.
Proof. Let Œª be the cardinality of the set of morphisms in a Œ∫-small category C, and
suppose there exists a parallel pair f, g: B ‚áíA of morphisms with f , g. By the universal
property of the Œª-indexed power (see Example 3.4.4), there are 2Œª distinct morphisms
B ‚ÜíAŒª whose components are either f or g. By Cantor‚Äôs diagonalization argument,
|C(B, AŒª)| ‚â•2Œª > Œª = | mor C| ,
contradicting the fact that C(B, AŒª) is a subset of mor C. If C instead has Œ∫-small copowers,
then a dual construction yields a similar contradiction.
‚ñ°
Proposition 3.5.9 reveals that it is possible for a preorder to have all limits or colimits
of unrestricted size: a poset P has this property as a category if and only if it is a complete
lattice.

3.8. Interactions between limits and colimits
Given a functor of several variables, it is natural to consider limits and colimits formed
separately in each variable. Assuming these objects exist, it follows from their universal
properties that limits commute with limits and colimits commute with colimits. Because
the universal properties of limits and colimits have opposite handedness, in the few cases
where limits and colimits commute with each other, this tends not to follow from formal
abstract nonsense, but instead most likely has something to do with how these objects are
constructed in the particular category in question.
To explore these issues, consider a functor F : I √ó J ‚ÜíC of two variables, which, by
Exercise 1.7.vii, may also be regarded as a functor F : I ‚ÜíCJ or as F : J ‚ÜíCI. If for
each i ‚ààI, limj‚ààJ F(i, j) exists, then by Proposition 3.3.9 these objects deÔ¨Åne the values

CHAPTER 4
Adjunctions
It appears . . . that there exists a kind of duality
between the tensor product and the . . . functor
Hom . . .
Daniel Kan, ‚ÄúAdjoint functors‚Äù [Kan58]
Vector spaces are sets equipped with a binary operation v + w (addition of vectors) and
a unary multiplication c ¬∑ v for each scalar c in the ground Ô¨Åeld k, satisfying certain axioms.
The passage from a vector space to its underlying set of ‚Äúvectors‚Äù is functorial, which is to
say there is a forgetful functor U : Vectk ‚ÜíSet that carries a linear transformation to its
underlying function.
A more interesting challenge is to reverse the direction of this translation and build a
vector space from a set S . Cardinality restrictions might prevent the direct use of S as the
set of vectors for a k-vector space, and even without this obstruction there is no natural way
to deÔ¨Åne vector addition in S . Instead, a more natural way to build a vector space is to let
the elements of S serve as a basis: vectors are then Ô¨Ånite formal sums1 k1s1 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ + knsn
with ki ‚ààk, si ‚ààS , and n ‚â•0. This deÔ¨Ånes a vector space k[S ] whose dimension is equal
to the cardinality of S , called the free vector space on the set S . Moreover, as our use
of the adjective ‚Äúnatural‚Äù would suggest, this construction is functorial, deÔ¨Åning a functor
k[‚àí]: Set ‚ÜíVectk: a function f : S ‚ÜíT induces a linear map k[f]: k[S ] ‚Üík[T] deÔ¨Åned
on the basis elements in the evident way.
There are many instances of free constructions in mathematics. One can deÔ¨Åne the
free (abelian) group on a set, the free ring on a set or on an abelian group, the free module
on an abelian group, the free category on a directed graph, and so on. Sometimes there are
competing notions of universal constructions: Does the free graph on a set have a single
edge between every pair of vertices or none? Does the free topological space on a set have
as many open sets as possible or as few? Other times there are none: For instance, there
is no ‚Äúfree Ô¨Åeld.‚Äù All of these constructions, and many others besides, are clariÔ¨Åed by the
concept of an adjunction, introduced by Kan [Kan58].
The universal property of the free vector space functor k[‚àí]: Set ‚ÜíVectk is expressed
by saying that it is left adjoint to the underlying set functor U : Vectk ‚ÜíSet: linear maps
k[S ] ‚ÜíV correspond naturally to functions S ‚ÜíU(V), which specify the image of the
basis vectors S ‚äÇk[S ]. By the Yoneda lemma, this universal property can be used to
deÔ¨Åne the action of the free vector space functor k[‚àí] on maps. The forgetful functor
U : Vectk ‚ÜíSet has no right adjoint, so in this setting there are no competing notions of
free construction.
1A precise way to deÔ¨Åne what is meant by this informal phrase is to say that a Ô¨Ånite formal sum is a Ô¨Ånitely
supported function œÜ: S ‚Üík, corresponding to the expression P
s‚ààS œÜ(s)s. In particular, two Ô¨Ånite sums are
considered to be identical when they diÔ¨Äer only up to reordering of terms, up to consolidating repeated instances
of the same term by adding their coeÔ¨Écients, or up to inclusion or deletion of terms whose coeÔ¨Écients are zero.

4. ADJUNCTIONS
In this chapter, we explore the general theory of adjoint functors, both in the abstract and
aided by a plethora of examples. Two equivalent deÔ¨Ånitions of an adjunction are presented
in ¬ß4.1 and ¬ß4.2, both of which oÔ¨Äer useful perspectives on the adjoint correspondence
between certain linear transformations and certain functions just mentioned. ¬ß4.3 explores
the forms of adjointness that arise between pairs of contravariant functors or between
(n + 1)-tuples of functors with n-variables. For instance, a two-variable adjunction encodes
the duality between the tensor product and hom bifunctors observed by Kan.
The basic calculus of adjunctions is developed in ¬ß4.4, with each result proven twice,
once using the Yoneda lemma and again via the techniques of ‚Äúformal category theory,‚Äù
to illustrate two important methods of categorical reasoning. In ¬ß4.5, we prove what is
perhaps the most frequently applied result in category theory: that right adjoint functors
preserve limits while left adjoint functors preserve colimits. This explains why limits tend
to be easier to construct than the formally dual colimit notions.
In particular, continuity is a necessary condition for a functor to admit a left adjoint,
and cocontinuity is necessary for the existence of a right adjoint. In ¬ß4.6, we develop
suÔ¨Écient conditions for a functor to have an adjoint, which can be used to construct the
missing adjoint functor in certain contexts.
4.1. Adjoint functors
Adjoint functors arise everywhere.

Example 4.1.6. The forgetful functor U : Top ‚ÜíSet admits both left and right adjoints.
The left adjoint constructs a topological space from a set S in such a way that continuous
maps from this space to another space T correspond naturally and bijectively to functions
S ‚ÜíU(T). The discrete topology on S has this universal property: writing D(S ) for this
discrete space, any function S ‚ÜíU(T) deÔ¨Ånes a continuous map D(S ) ‚ÜíT.

indiscrete space, any function U(T) ‚ÜíS deÔ¨Ånes a continuous function T ‚ÜíI(S ). The

Example 4.1.13. The forgetful functor U : Cat ‚ÜíDirGraph admits a left adjoint F,
deÔ¨Åning the free category on a directed graph. A directed graph G consists of a set V of
vertices, a set E of edges, and two functions s, t: E ‚áíV deÔ¨Åning the source and target of
each directed edge. The free category on G has V as its set of objects. The set of morphisms
consists of identities for each vertex together with Ô¨Ånite ‚Äúpaths‚Äù of edges. Composition is
deÔ¨Åned by concatenation of paths.
The adjunction supplies a natural bijection between functors F(G) ‚ÜíC and morphisms
G ‚ÜíU(C) of directed graphs. A functor F(G) ‚ÜíC, or equally a directed graph morphism
G ‚ÜíU(C), deÔ¨Ånes a diagram in C with no commutativity requirements, since directed
graphs do not encode composites. The data of such a diagram is uniquely determined by the
images of the vertices and edges in the directed graph G. These edges form atomic arrows
in the category F(G), admitting no non-trivial factorizations. For instance, as described in

4.1. ADJOINT FUNCTORS

Example 1.1.4(iv), the category œâ is free on the directed graph whose vertices are indexed
by natural numbers and with edges n ‚Üín + 1. On account of the adjunction, an œâ-indexed
diagram is deÔ¨Åned by specifying only the images of the atomic arrows, from an ordinal to
its successor.

Lemma 4.2.3. Given an adjunction F ‚ä£G, there is a natural transformation œµ : FG ‚áí1D,
called the counit of the adjunction, whose component œµd : FGd ‚Üíd at d is deÔ¨Åned to be
the transpose of the identity morphism 1Gd.
Example 4.2.4. The left adjoint F to the forgetful functor U : Group ‚ÜíSet deÔ¨Ånes the
free group on a set S . Elements of the group F(S ) are Ô¨Ånite ‚Äúwords‚Äù whose ‚Äúletters‚Äù are
elements s of S and formal ‚Äúinverses‚Äù s‚àí1, modulo some evident relations. The empty
word serves as the identity element and multiplication is by concatenation; the relations, in
particular, identify ss‚àí1 and s‚àí1s with the empty word.
The component of the unit of the adjunction at a set S is the function Œ∑S : S ‚ÜíUF(S )
that sends an element s ‚ààS to the corresponding singleton word. The component of the
counit of the adjunction at a group G is the group homomorphism œµG : FU(G) ‚ÜíG that
sends a word, whose letters are elements of the group G and formal inverses, to the product
of those symbols, interpreted using the multiplication, inverses, and identity present in the
group G.
A similar description, with the unit deÔ¨Åning some sort of ‚Äúsingleton‚Äù map and the
counit deÔ¨Åning some form of ‚Äúevaluation‚Äù homomorphism, can be given for many of the
other adjunctions listed in Example 4.1.10.
In the terminology of DeÔ¨Ånition 2.3.3, the components of the unit and the counit deÔ¨Åne
universal elements characterizing the natural isomorphisms

Lemmas 4.2.2 and 4.2.3 prove that any adjunction has a unit and a counit. Conversely,
if F : C ‚áÑD: G are opposing functors equipped with natural transformations Œ∑: 1C ‚áíGF

natural bijection D(Fc, d)  C(c,Gd), exhibiting F and G as adjoint functors. That is, the
notion of adjunction may be redeÔ¨Åned as follows:

4.3. Contravariant and multivariable adjoint functors
Kan‚Äôs original exploration of adjoint functors also considered adjoint relationships
involving functors that are contravariant or have more than one variable [Kan58]. Indeed,
these examples provided the central motivation for Kan‚Äôs discovery, at least according to an
apocryphal account of a question he posed to Eilenberg in a homological algebra seminar
at Columbia University [Mar09, ¬ß4.2]:
kan: You have explained how the tensor product can be deÔ¨Åned
in terms of the hom functor. Can the hom instead be deÔ¨Åned
in terms of the tensor product?
eilenberg: No, of course not. That‚Äôs absurd.
In fact, Kan‚Äôs intuition was correct. By the Yoneda lemma, any adjoint functor determines
its adjoints up to natural isomorphism. Example 4.3.11 explains how to deÔ¨Åne the hom
bifunctor from the tensor product.
Contravariant adjoint functors require little special consideration, arising simply as an
application of the principle of duality. DeÔ¨Ånition 4.1.1 can be dualized in three ways, by
replacing C, D, or both C and D by their opposite categories. The latter dualization recovers
the original notion of adjunction, with G: Dop ‚ÜíCop left adjoint to F : Cop ‚ÜíDop if and
only if F ‚ä£G. In particular, any theorem about left adjoints has a dual theorem about right
adjoints, which is a very useful duality principle for adjunctions. The other two dualizations
lead to new types of adjoint functors.
Definition 4.3.1. A pair of contravariant functors F : Cop ‚ÜíD and G: Dop ‚ÜíC are
mutually left adjoint if there exists a natural isomorphism
D(Fc, d)  C(Gd, c) ,

4.3. CONTRAVARIANT AND MULTIVARIABLE ADJOINT FUNCTORS
127
or mutually right adjoint if there exists a natural isomorphism
D(d, Fc)  C(c,Gd) .
Dualizing DeÔ¨Ånition 4.2.5, a pair of mutual left adjoints F : Cop ‚ÜíD and G: Dop ‚ÜíC
come equipped with a pair of ‚Äúcounit‚Äù natural transformations GF ‚áí1C and FG ‚áí1D,
while a pair of mutual right adjoints F : Cop ‚ÜíD and G: Dop ‚ÜíC come equipped with
a pair of ‚Äúunit‚Äù natural transformations 1C ‚áíGF and 1D ‚áíFG. The formulation of the
triangle identities in each case is left to Exercise 4.3.i.
Mutual right adjoints between preorders form what is sometimes called an antitone
Galois connection.

Definition 4.3.9. A cartesian closed category is a category C with Ô¨Ånite products in

Example 4.3.10 (a convenient category of spaces). The category Top is not cartesian
closed. However, a famous paper of Steenrod proves that there exists a convenient category
of topological spaces that is complete, cocomplete, cartesian closed, and suÔ¨Éciently large
so as to contain the CW complexes [Ste67]. Steenrod‚Äôs convenient category is a subcategory
of the category Haus of HausdorÔ¨Äspaces and continuous functions. Previous researchers
had argued that a ‚Äúconvenient category of spaces‚Äù satisfying Steenrod‚Äôs list of conditions
did not exist, but Steenrod notes:
The arguments are based on a blind adherence to the customary
deÔ¨Ånitions of the standard operations. These deÔ¨Ånitions are suitable
for the category of HausdorÔ¨Äspaces, but they need not be for a
subcategory. The categorical viewpoint enables us to defrost these
deÔ¨Ånitions and bend them a bit.

130
4. ADJUNCTIONS
What he means is that the ‚Äúproduct‚Äù on a yet-to-be-deÔ¨Åned subcategory cgHaus should be
the categorical product of DeÔ¨Ånition 3.1.9, which, as it turns out, is not preserved by the
full inclusion cgHaus ,‚ÜíHaus.
Objects in cgHaus are compactly generated HausdorÔ¨Äspaces: HausdorÔ¨Äspaces X
with the property that any subset A ‚äÇX that intersects each compact subset K ‚äÇX in a
closed subset A‚à©K is itself closed in X. The inclusion cgHaus ,‚ÜíHaus has a right adjoint
k: Haus ‚ÜícgHaus called k-iÔ¨Åcation: the space k(X) reÔ¨Ånes the topology on X by adding
to the collection of closed sets those subsets A ‚äÇX whose intersections with all compact
subsets K are closed.
By Exercise 4.6.ii and the dual of Proposition 4.5.15, the presence of this adjoint
implies that cgHaus is complete and cocomplete. The product on cgHaus is the k-iÔ¨Åcation
of the product on Haus; this latter product is preserved by the inclusion Haus ,‚ÜíTop. The
pointwise right adjoint is given by the following construction of function spaces Map(X, Y):
the underlying set of Map(X, Y) is the set of continuous maps X ‚ÜíY, and the topology is
the k-iÔ¨Åcation of the compact-open topology. See [Ste67] for a proof.
Other categories admit a hom bifunctor that is not necessarily right adjoint to the
cartesian product.
The most famous of these are the ‚Äútensor ‚ä£hom‚Äù adjunctions that
inspired Kan:

4.4. The calculus of adjunctions
Perhaps the purpose of categorical algebra is to
show that which is formal is formally formal.
J. Peter May, [May01]
This section introduces the basic calculus of adjoint functors: proving that adjoints are
unique up to unique natural isomorphism, that adjunctions can be composed and whiskered,
and that any equivalence can be promoted to an adjoint equivalence in which the inverse
equivalences are also adjoints, with either choice of handedness.
What further unites
these results is that they all admit ‚Äúformal categorical‚Äù6 proofs, meaning that they can be
proven syntactically using objects (categories), morphisms (functors), and morphisms of
morphisms (natural transformations), which can be composed horizontally and vertically in
the sense described in ¬ß1.7. As our interest is as much in these categorical proof techniques
as in the results themselves, we give secondary proofs of these results, to illustrate alternate
Yoneda-style arguments, and brieÔ¨Çy comment on the merits of each approach.

As in the case of the other results in this section, this can be proven by appealing to
either DeÔ¨Ånition 4.1.1 or DeÔ¨Ånition 4.2.5. Rather than spell out the details, which are left
for the exercises, we instead sketch a third proof.
DeÔ¨Ånition 4.2.5 reveals that an adjunction can be deÔ¨Åned internally to the 2-category
CAT introduced in DeÔ¨Ånition 1.7.8: an adjunction consists of a pair of objects, a pair of
1-morphisms, and a pair of 2-morphisms satisfying certain composition relations. Now
a 2-functor is a morphism between 2-categories: a map on objects, 1-morphisms, and
2-morphisms preserving composition and identities at all levels. There is a 2-dimensional
analog of Lemma 1.6.5‚Äîthat functors preserve commutative diagrams‚Äîwhich says that 2-
functors preserve ‚Äú2-dimensional diagrams‚Äù including, in particular, adjunctions. Ignoring
size issues, Proposition 4.4.6 is an immediate corollary of the fact that
(‚àí)J : CAT ‚ÜíCAT
and
E(‚àí) : Catop ‚ÜíCAT
deÔ¨Åne 2-functors for any small category J and locally small category E.
Remark 4.4.7. The ‚Äúsyntactic‚Äù proofs of Propositions 4.4.1, 4.4.4, 4.4.5, and 4.4.6 (the
last of these left to Exercise 4.4.ii) apply immediately to adjunctions deÔ¨Åned internally to
any 2-category, in place of Cat. This suggests a reason to prefer these proofs to the alternate
‚ÄúYoneda-style‚Äù arguments, which apply only to Cat. However, using analogous arguments
to those made in ¬ß3.4, these ‚ÄúYoneda-style‚Äù arguments can be bootstrapped from Cat to a
general 2-category. As (co)limits in a general category are deÔ¨Åned representably in terms
of limits in Set, so too‚Äîagain by the Yoneda lemma‚Äîare adjunctions and equivalences
in a 2-category are deÔ¨Åned representably in terms of adjunctions and equivalences in Cat.
This line of reasoning supports the category theorist‚Äôs joke that ‚Äúall theorems are Yoneda.‚Äù

4.5. Adjunctions, limits, and colimits
In this section, we explore the interaction between adjoint functors and limits and
colimits. Our Ô¨Årst result provides an adjoint characterization of completeness and cocom-
pleteness of a category C with regard to diagrams of a Ô¨Åxed shape J.
Proposition 4.5.1. A category C admits all limits of diagrams indexed by a small category
J if and only if the constant diagram functor ‚àÜ: C ‚ÜíCJ admits a right adjoint, and admits
all colimits of J-indexed diagrams if and only if ‚àÜadmits a left adjoint:
C
‚àÜ
/ CJ .
colim
‚ä•
~

‚ä•
_
When these adjoints exist, they deÔ¨Åne the limit and colimit functors introduced in
Proposition 3.6.1. Recall that the axiom of choice is needed to deÔ¨Åne the action of the limit
or colimit functor on objects.
Proof. These dual statements follow immediately from the deÔ¨Åning universal proper-
ties of a limit and colimit. For c ‚ààC and F ‚ààCJ, the hom-set CJ(‚àÜc, F) is the set of natural
transformations from the constant J-diagram at c to the diagram F. This is precisely the set
of cones over F with summit c, as deÔ¨Åned in 3.1.2. There is an object lim F ‚ààC together
with an isomorphism
CJ(‚àÜc, F)  C(c, lim F)
that is natural in c ‚ààC if and only if this limit exists. If such natural isomorphisms exist for
all diagrams F ‚ààCJ, then Proposition 4.3.4 applies to extend the objects lim F to a limit
functor lim: CJ ‚ÜíC.
‚ñ°
Proposition 4.5.1 reveals that limits assemble into a right adjoint to the constant
diagram functor. The reason that limits appear on the right is because limits are deÔ¨Åned as
representations for contravariant functors: the universal property of the limit of F : J ‚ÜíC
characterizes the functor C(‚àí, lim F).
Similarly, the value of a right adjoint functor G: D ‚ÜíC on an object d ‚ààD is
determined by a characterization of the contravariant representable functor C(‚àí,Gd). Based
on these observations, one might expect right adjoints to interact nicely with limits and
indeed this is the case:
Theorem 4.5.2 (RAPL). Right adjoints preserve limits.

4.5. ADJUNCTIONS, LIMITS, AND COLIMITS
139
The forgetful functors of Example 4.1.10 carry any limits that exist in the categories
of groups, rings, modules, and so forth to corresponding limits of their underlying sets.
Indeed, in ¬ß5.6, we show that these forgetful functors create, and not merely preserve, all
limits. The dual result, that left adjoints preserve colimits, is less directly useful in such
contexts, characterizing only those colimit constructions involving ‚Äúfree‚Äù diagrams. For
instance:
Corollary 4.5.7. The free group on the set X ‚äîY is the free product of the free groups on
the sets X and Y.
The free product of not-necessarily free groups will be constructed in ¬ß5.6 using
language that also describes the construction of the coproducts in the categories of abelian
groups, rings, and modules, among many others.
Theorems 4.5.2 and 4.5.3 also have important applications to homological algebra.
Corollary 4.5.8. For any R-S bimodule M, the tensor product M ‚äóS ‚àíis right exact.
Before proving Corollary 4.5.8, we should explain its statement. The term ‚Äúright exact‚Äù
comes from homological algebra, which studies functors between abelian categories. An
abstract deÔ¨Ånition is given in DeÔ¨Ånition E.5.1, but by a powerful result, Theorem E.5.2, it
suÔ¨Éces to declare that a category is abelian if it is a full subcategory of category of modules
that contains the zero object and is closed under direct sums, kernels, and cokernels.8
The following general deÔ¨Ånitions of left and right exactness makes sense for any
functor, not necessarily between abelian categories.
Definition 4.5.9. A functor is right exact if it preserves Ô¨Ånite colimits and left exact if it
preserves Ô¨Ånite limits.
The following proposition connects DeÔ¨Ånition 4.5.9 to the notions of left and right
exactness that are used in homological algebra.
Proposition 4.5.10. A functor F : A ‚ÜíB between abelian categories is left exact if and
only if it preserves direct sums and kernels. For such functors, if
0 ‚ÜíA ‚ÜíA‚Ä≤ ‚ÜíA‚Ä≤‚Ä≤
is an exact sequence in A, then
0 ‚ÜíFA ‚ÜíFA‚Ä≤ ‚ÜíFA‚Ä≤‚Ä≤
is exact in B.
Proof. Direct sums and kernels are both Ô¨Ånite limits, so if F is left exact, these
constructions are preserved. Conversely, Theorem 3.4.12 proves that all Ô¨Ånite limits can be
built from Ô¨Ånite products and equalizers. In an abelian category, the equalizer of a parallel
pair of maps f, g: A ‚áíA‚Ä≤ is the kernel of the map f ‚àíg: A ‚ÜíA‚Ä≤. A functor that preserves
direct sums also preserves diÔ¨Äerences of maps, so if F preserves direct sums and kernels it
preserves all Ô¨Ånite limits.

The following result explains our particular interest in reÔ¨Çective subcategories. The
presence of a left adjoint to the inclusion of a full subcategory provides complete information
about limits and colimits in that subcategory:
Proposition 4.5.15. If D ,‚ÜíC is a reÔ¨Çective subcategory, then:
(i) The inclusion D ,‚ÜíC creates all limits that C admits.
(ii) D has all colimits that C admits, formed by applying the reÔ¨Çector to the colimit in C.
By Theorems 4.5.2 and 4.5.3, if D has limits or colimits, they must be constructed in
the way described in Proposition 4.5.15: limits are preserved by the inclusion D ,‚ÜíC and
colimits of diagrams in D, regarded as diagrams in C, are preserved by L: C ‚ÜíD. The
real content of this result is that these (co)limits necessarily exist in D. A special case of
this completes some unÔ¨Ånished business from ¬ß3.5:
Corollary 4.5.16. Cat is complete and cocomplete.
Proof. By Proposition 3.3.9, Set‚àÜop is complete and cocomplete, with limits and col-
imits deÔ¨Åned objectwise in Set. Proposition 4.5.15 implies that the reÔ¨Çective subcategory
Cat inherits these limits, deÔ¨Åned objectwise in Set, and also these colimits, deÔ¨Åned by
applying the homotopy category functor to the colimit in Set‚àÜop.
‚ñ°
Proposition 4.5.15(i) appears as Corollary 5.6.6, where this result is deduced as a
special case of the more general Theorem 5.6.5.
Proof of 4.5.15(ii). For clarity, write i: D ,‚ÜíC for the inclusion, the right adjoint
to the reÔ¨Çector L. Consider a diagram F : J ‚ÜíD and let Œª: iF ‚áíc be a colimit cone
for the diagram iF in C. By Theorem 4.5.3, the left adjoint L sends this to a colimit cone
LŒª: LiF ‚áíLc in D. Now the counit supplies a natural isomorphism Li  1D. Composing
with this natural isomorphism yields a colimit cone F  LiF ‚áíLc for the original diagram
in D.

4.6. Existence of adjoint functors
Does the inclusion Ring ,‚ÜíRng of the category of unital rings into the category of
possibly non-unital rings have any adjoints? A Ô¨Årst strategy to probe a question of this
form might be called the ‚Äúinitial and terminal objects test‚Äù: by Theorems 4.5.2 and 4.5.3, a
functor admitting a left adjoint must necessarily preserve all limits while a functor admitting
a right adjoint must necessarily preserve all colimits. The ring Z is initial in Ring but not
in Rng, so there can be no right adjoint. The zero ring is terminal in both categories, so a

144
4. ADJUNCTIONS
left adjoint to the inclusion might be possible. Indeed, Ring has all limits and the inclusion
preserves them.10
The search for a left adjoint to Ring ,‚ÜíRng might be thought of as some sort
of formulaic (that is to say functorial) optimization problem, whose aim is to adjoin a
multiplicative unit to a possibly non-unital ring R in the ‚Äúmost eÔ¨Écient way possible.‚Äù
The Yoneda lemma can be used to make this intuition precise. To deÔ¨Åne the value of a
hypothetical left adjoint on a possibly non-unital ring R, we seek a unital ring R‚àótogether
with a natural isomorphism
Ring(R‚àó, S )  Rng(R, S )
for all unital rings S . Note that even if R has a unit, the ring R‚àówill still diÔ¨Äer from R
because homomorphisms in Ring must preserve units, while homomorphisms in Rng need
not.
To deÔ¨Åne this natural isomorphism is to deÔ¨Åne a representation R‚àófor the functor
Rng(R, ‚àí): Ring ‚ÜíSet. By Proposition 2.4.8, a representation deÔ¨Ånes an initial object in
the category of elements
R
Rng(R, ‚àí). Objects in this category are homomorphisms R ‚ÜíS
whose codomain is a unital ring; morphisms are commutative triangles
R

 
S
/ S ‚Ä≤
whose leg opposite R is a unital ring homomorphism but whose legs with domain R do
not necessarily preserve the multiplicative unit, if R happens to have one. This category
of elements is isomorphic to the comma category R‚ÜìRing of non-unital homomorphisms
from R to a unital ring. The optimization problem is solved if we can Ô¨Ånd a unital ring R‚àó
and ring homomorphism R ‚ÜíR‚àóthat is initial in this category.11
The same line of reasoning proves the following general result.
Lemma 4.6.1. A functor U : A ‚ÜíS admits a left adjoint if and only if for each s ‚ààS the
comma category s‚ÜìU has an initial object.

h: a ‚Üía‚Ä≤ ‚ààA so that the evident triangle commutes in S.

deÔ¨Ånes an initial object Œ∑s : s ‚ÜíUFs in this category; see Proposition 2.4.8 or Exercise

Recall that a limit-preserving functor is called continuous; a colimit-preserving functor
is called cocontinuous. Lemma 4.6.1 reduces the problem of Ô¨Ånding a left adjoint to a
continuous functor U : A ‚ÜíS to the problem of Ô¨Ånding an initial object in the comma
category s ‚ÜìU deÔ¨Åned for each s ‚ààS. This comma category, as the category of elements
for S(s, U‚àí): A ‚ÜíSet, comes with a canonical forgetful functor Œ†: s‚ÜìU ‚ÜíA that carries
an object s ‚ÜíUa to the object a.
Lemma 4.6.2. For any functor U : A ‚ÜíS and object s ‚ààS, the associated forgetful
functor Œ†: s‚ÜìU ‚ÜíA strictly creates the limit of any diagram whose limit exists in A and is
preserved by U. In particular, if A is complete and U is continuous, then s‚ÜìU is complete.
Proof. Lemma 4.6.2 is a special case of Exercise 3.4.iii, or can be proven directly via
a straightforward extension of the argument used to prove Proposition 3.3.8.
‚ñ°
Lemma 4.6.2 can be used to produce an initial object in s ‚ÜìU. By Lemma 3.7.1,
an initial object is equally the limit of the identity functor. Applying Lemma 4.6.2, a
limit of the identity functor on s ‚ÜìU exists if and only if the limit of the forgetful functor
Œ†: s‚ÜìU ‚ÜíA exists in A.
This line of reasoning seems to imply that all continuous functors whose domains are
complete should admit left adjoints. This is not the case: the problem is that s ‚ÜìU is not
in general a small category, so even if A admits all small limits it may not admit a limit of
the large diagram Œ†: s‚ÜìU ‚ÜíA. The adjoint functor theorems, the two most common of
which are discussed here, supply conditions under which this large limit can be reduced to
a small one that A possesses.

Theorem 4.6.3 (General Adjoint Functor Theorem). Let U : A ‚ÜíS be a continuous

The solution set condition says exactly that {fi : s ‚ÜíUai} is a set of jointly weakly
initial objects in the category s‚ÜìU.
Definition 4.6.4.
(i) An object c in a category C is weakly initial if for every x ‚ààC there exists a morphism
c ‚Üíx (but possibly more than one).

x ‚ààC there exists some ci ‚ààŒ¶ for which there exists a morphism ci ‚Üíx.

Definition 4.6.7. A generating set or generator for a category C is a set Œ¶ of objects
that can distinguish between distinct parallel morphisms in the following sense: given
f, g: x ‚áíy, if f , g then there exists some h: c ‚Üíx with c ‚ààŒ¶ so that fh , gh. A
cogenerating set in C is a generating set in Cop.

4. ADJUNCTIONS
Definition 4.6.8. A subobject of an object c ‚ààC is a monomorphism c‚Ä≤ ‚Ü£c with
codomain c. Isomorphic subobjects, that is, subobjects c‚Ä≤ ‚Ü£c ‚Ü¢c‚Ä≤‚Ä≤ with a commuting
isomorphism c‚Ä≤  c‚Ä≤‚Ä≤, are typically identiÔ¨Åed.
Definition 4.6.9. The intersection of a family of subobjects of c is the limit of the diagram
of monomorphisms with codomain c.
The induced map from the limit to c is again a monomorphism. By Lemma 1.2.11(ii),
the legs of the limit cone are also monomorphisms. Thus, the intersection is the minimal
subobject that is contained in every member of the family of subobjects.
Theorem 4.6.10 (Special Adjoint Functor Theorem). Let U : A ‚ÜíS be a continuous
functor whose domain is complete and whose domain and codomain are locally small.
Furthermore, if A has a small cogenerating set and every collection of subobjects of a Ô¨Åxed
object in A admits an intersection, then U admits a left adjoint.
In many categories, each object admits only a set‚Äôs worth of subobjects up to isomor-
phism, in which case completeness implies that every collection of subobjects admits an
intersection. The purpose for these hypotheses is that they are used to construct the initial
objects sought for in Lemma 4.6.1.

The adjoint functor theorems have a number of corollaries.
Corollary 4.6.13. Suppose C is locally small, complete, has a small cogenerating set,
and has the property that every collection of subobjects of a Ô¨Åxed object has an intersection.
Then C is also cocomplete.
Proof. For any small category J, if C is locally small, then CJ is again locally small.
The constant diagram functor ‚àÜ: C ‚ÜíCJ preserves limits by Exercise 3.3.vi. Applying
Theorem 4.6.10, ‚àÜhas a left adjoint, which by Proposition 4.5.1 demonstrates that C has
all J-shaped colimits.

and has the property that every collection of subobjects of a Ô¨Åxed object has an intersection.
Then any continuous functor F : C ‚ÜíSet is representable.
Proof. By Theorem 4.6.10, F has a left adjoint L: Set ‚ÜíC. In particular, there is a

4. ADJUNCTIONS
These corollaries aside, the Adjoint Functor Theorems 4.6.3 and 4.6.10 tend to be both
useless and widely applicable. Their ineÔ¨Äectualness stems from the fact that solution sets
or coseparating sets tend to be hard to Ô¨Ånd in practice. At the same time, a very simple
general characterization of adjoint functors between a large family of categories can be
obtained from these theorems.
Definition 4.6.16. Let Œ∫ be a regular cardinal.12 A locally small category C is locally
Œ∫-presentable if it is cocomplete and if it has a set of objects S so that:
(i) Every object in C can be written as a colimit of a diagram valued in the subcategory
spanned by the objects in S .
(ii) For each object s ‚ààS , the functor C(s, ‚àí): C ‚ÜíSet preserves Œ∫-Ô¨Åltered colimits.
A functor between locally Œ∫-presentable categories is accessible if it preserves Œ∫-Ô¨Åltered
colimits.
For example, a large variety of categories whose objects are sets equipped with some
sort of ‚Äúalgebraic‚Äù structure are locally Ô¨Ånitely presentable (meaning locally œâ-presentable);
see DeÔ¨Ånition 5.5.5. A locally Œ∫-presentable category is also locally Œª-presentable for any
Œª > Œ∫.
Between locally presentable categories, meaning categories that are locally
Œ∫-presentable for some Œ∫, the adjoint functor theorem takes a particularly appealing form.
Theorem 4.6.17. A functor F : C ‚ÜíD between locally presentable categories
(i) admits a right adjoint if and only if it is cocontinuous.
(ii) admits a left adjoint if and only if it is continuous and accessible.
Proof sketch. Theorem 4.6.3 proves (ii): any accessible functor satisÔ¨Åes the solution
set condition; see [AR94, 1.66].
The dual of Theorem 4.6.10 proves (i): the colimit-generating set of objects S deÔ¨Ånes
a generator for C. A hard theorem proves that any object in a locally presentable category
admits at most a set‚Äôs worth of epimorphic quotients, up to isomorphism [AR94, 1.58].
Cocompleteness then implies that cointersections of quotient objects always exist.
‚ñ°
The categories Ring and Rng are locally presentable and the inclusion is accessible,
and so Theorem 4.6.17 implies that a left adjoint to the inclusion Ring ,‚ÜíRng exists. But
this general result does not tell us anything useful about how it is constructed. Instead, we
sketch a direct construction of the left adjoint that will preview some of the themes from
the next chapter.

Monads and their Algebras
A monad is just a monoid in the category of
endofunctors, what‚Äôs the problem?
Philip Walder, Ô¨Åctional attribution by James Iry
[Iry09]
Consider an adjunction
C
F
/
‚ä•
D

o
Œ∑: 1C ‚áíUF,
œµ : FU ‚áí1D
from the perspective of the category C (‚Äúhome‚Äù), in ignorance of the category D (‚Äúabroad‚Äù).
From C, only part of the data of the adjunction is visible: the composite endofunctor
UF : C ‚ÜíC but neither U nor F individually, and the unit natural transformation but
only a whiskered version of the counit. In this chapter, we study the ‚Äúshadow‚Äù cast by
an adjunction on the codomain of its right adjoint, the data of which forms a monad on
C. Monads are interesting in their own right but additionally so because, in unexpectedly
common situations, the category D can be recovered from the monad on C underlying
the adjunction. When this is the case, objects of D can be represented as algebras for
the monad on C. In general, a monad can be thought of as a syntactic representation of
algebraic structure that is potentially borne by objects in the category on which it acts.1
The general deÔ¨Ånition of a monad is introduced in ¬ß5.1 together with a range of
examples. As hinted here, any adjunction gives rise to a monad. The converse question,
of constructing an adjunction that presents a given monad, is investigated in ¬ß5.2, where
two universal solutions to this problem are described: the Kleisli and Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore
categories, which can be constructed for any monad, also arise naturally in several areas of
mathematics.
The Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore category, also called the category of algebras for the monad,
is our focus for the remainder of the chapter. In ¬ß5.3, we pose the question of identifying
whether a right adjoint functor U : D ‚ÜíC is equivalent to the forgetful functor from the
category of algebras of its monad, in which case the functor is called monadic. When D is
equivalent to the category of algebras, there are a number of pleasing consequences. The
Ô¨Årst consequence, proven in ¬ß5.4, is that every algebra admits a canonical presentation as
a quotient of free algebras. This construction generalizes the presentation of a group via
generators and relations.
The monadicity theorem, proven in ¬ß5.5, establishes necessary and suÔ¨Écient conditions
for a right adjoint to be monadic. The concluding ¬ß5.6 explains our interest in recognizing
1Dually, the ‚Äúshadow‚Äù cast by an adjunction on the codomain of its left adjoint deÔ¨Ånes a comonad, encoding
coalgebraic structure, examples of which are somewhat less common. So that the exposition aligns with the most
prominent examples, this dual theory will largely remain unacknowledged.

5. MONADS AND THEIR ALGEBRAS
categories of algebras: this characterization has signiÔ¨Åcant implications for the construction
of limits and colimits, which were hinted at in Chapters 3 and 4. For example, monoids,
groups, rings, modules over a Ô¨Åxed ring, sets with an action of a Ô¨Åxed group, pointed sets,
compact HausdorÔ¨Äspaces, lattices, and so on all arise as algebras for a monad on the
category of sets. Fields do not, which explains why the category of Ô¨Åelds shares few of the
properties that are common to the categories just described.
5.1. Monads from adjunctions
The abstract deÔ¨Ånition of a monad is relatively simple to state:

Remark 5.1.2. The diagrams in DeÔ¨Ånition 5.1.1 are reminiscent of the diagrams in DeÔ¨Å-
nition 1.6.3. This is no coincidence. Monads, like (topological) monoids, unital rings, and
k-algebras, are all instances of monoids in a monoidal category. A monoidal category V is
a category equipped with a binary functor V √ó V ‚ÜíV and a unit object, together with some
additional coherence natural isomorphisms satisfying conditions that are described in ¬ßE.2.
A monad on C is precisely a monoid in the monoidal category CC of endofunctors on C,
where the binary functor CC √ó CC ‚ÜíCC is composition, and the unit object is the identity
endofunctor 1C ‚ààCC. The category of endofunctors is a strict monoidal category, in which
the coherence natural isomorphisms can be taken to be identities. Nonetheless, the notion
of monoid in a monoidal category is rather more complicated than the notion of monad on
a category, which is the point of the following joke from Iry‚Äôs ‚ÄúA Brief, Incomplete, and
Mostly Wrong History of Programming Languages‚Äù [Iry09]:
1990‚ÄîA committee formed by Simon Peyton-Jones, Paul Hudak,
Philip Wadler, . . . creates Haskell, a pure, non-strict, functional lan-
guage. Haskell gets some resistance due to the complexity of using
monads to control side eÔ¨Äects. Wadler tries to appease critics by ex-
plaining that ‚Äúa monad is a monoid in the category of endofunctors,
what‚Äôs the problem?‚Äù
One source of intuition is that a monad is the ‚Äúshadow‚Äù cast by an adjunction on the
category appearing as the codomain of the right adjoint. Consider the adjunction

A function from A to the power set of B, or equally a function from B to the power
set of A, can be encoded as a function A √ó B ‚Üí‚Ñ¶, i.e., as a subset of A √ó B. The
induced double power set monad takes a set A to P2A. The components of the
unit are the ‚Äúprinciple ultraÔ¨Ålter‚Äù functions Œ∑A : A ‚ÜíP2A that send an element a to
the set of subsets of A that contain a. The components of the multiplication take
a set of sets of sets of subsets to the set of subsets of A with the property that one
of the sets of sets of subsets is the set of all sets of subsets of A that include that
particular subset as an element.3 A similar monad can be deÔ¨Åned with any other
set in place of the two-element set ‚Ñ¶; in computer science contexts, these are called
continuation monads. This construction can also be generalized to other cartesian
closed categories. For instance, there is a similar double dual monad on Vectk.

makes use of the fact that f‚àó: PA ‚ÜíPB is the direct image (rather than inverse

the direct image function, as a left adjoint, preserves unions.

(iv) The Giry monad acts on the category Meas of measurable spaces and measurable

preimage of any measurable subset of B is a measurable subset of A. The Giry
monad sends a measurable space A to the measurable space Prob(A) of probability
measures on A, equipped with the smallest œÉ-algebra so that, for each measurable
subset X ‚äÇA, the evaluation function evX : Prob(A) ‚ÜíI is measurable. The unit is
the measurable function Œ∑A : A ‚ÜíProb(A) that sends each element a ‚ààA to the Dirac
measure, which assigns a subset the probability 1 if it contains a and 0 otherwise.
The multiplication is deÔ¨Åned using integration; see [Gir82] or [Ave16] for details.

5. MONADS AND THEIR ALGEBRAS
Definition 5.1.6. A comonad on C is a monad on Cop: explicitly, a comonad consists of an
endofunctor K : C ‚ÜíC together with natural transformations œµ : K ‚áí1C and Œ¥: K ‚áíK2
so that the diagrams dual to DeÔ¨Ånition 5.1.1 commute in CC.
A comonad is a comonoid in the category of endofunctors of C. By the dual to Lemma
5.1.3, any adjunction induces a comonad on the domain of its right adjoint.
Example 5.1.7. A monad on a preorder (P, ‚â§) is given by an order-preserving function
T : P ‚ÜíP that is so that p ‚â§T p and T 2p ‚â§T p. If P is a poset, so that isomorphic objects
are equal, these two conditions imply that T 2p = T p. An order-preserving function T so
that p ‚â§T p and T 2p = T p is called a closure operator. Dually, a comonad on a poset
category (P, ‚â§) deÔ¨Ånes a kernel operator: an order-preserving function K so that Kp ‚â§p
and Kp = K2p.
For example, the poset PX of subsets of a topological space X admits a closure operator
TA = A, where A is the closure of A ‚äÇX, and a kernel operator KA = A‚ó¶, where A‚ó¶is the
interior of A ‚äÇX.4

5.2. Adjunctions from monads
We have seen, in Lemma 5.1.3, that any adjunction presents a monad on the category
serving as the domain of its left adjoint. A natural question is whether all monads arise this
way. For instance, is there any adjunction that gives rise to the power set monad on Set?
Perhaps surprisingly, the answer is yes: any monad T on a category C can be recovered
from two (typically distinct) adjunctions that are moreover universal with this property. The
initial such adjunction, in a category to be introduced shortly, is between C and the Kleisli

of n varying-length lists of elements of A. Commutativity of (5.2.7) demands that
the result of applying the (m1 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ + mn)-ary operation to the concatenated list is the
same as applying the n-ary operation to the results of applying the mi-ary operations
to each sublist.
Recall that a monoid is a set A with an associative binary operation A2 ‚ÜíA and
a unit element e ‚ààA. A monoid deÔ¨Ånes an algebra for the free monoid monad: the
nullary operation 1 ‚ÜíA picks out the unit element e, the unary operation A ‚ÜíA is
necessarily the identity, the binary operation A2 ‚ÜíA is given, and the operations of
higher arity are determined by iterating the binary operation; associativity implies
that this is well-deÔ¨Åned.
Conversely, any algebra for the free monoid monad deÔ¨Ånes a monoid, whose unit
and binary operations are deÔ¨Åned to be the components Œ±0 : 1 ‚ÜíA and Œ±2 : A2 ‚ÜíA
of the map Œ±: `
n‚â•0 An ‚ÜíA. The associativity condition (5.2.7) implies in particular
that the ternary product of three elements a1, a2, a3 ‚ààA is equal to both of the iterated
binary products (a1 ¬∑ a2) ¬∑ a3 and a1 ¬∑ (a2 ¬∑ a3):
Œ±2(Œ±2(a1, a2), a3) = Œ±3(a1, a2, a3) = Œ±2(a1, Œ±2(a2, a3)) .
The square (5.2.7) also implies that the composite of the binary operation with the
nullary operation in one of its inputs is equal to the identity unary operation.

5. MONADS AND THEIR ALGEBRAS
By Exercise 5.2.i, algebra morphisms correspond precisely to monoid homo-
morphisms. Thus, we conclude that the category of algebras for the free monoid
monad is isomorphic to the category Monoid of associative unital monoids.
(iv) An algebra for the closure closure operator7 on the poset of subsets of a topological
space X is exactly a closed subset of X. Dually, a coalgebra8 for the interior kernel
operator is exactly an open subset.

Proposition 5.2.12 implies, in particular, that there is a unique functor from the Kleisli

Lemma 5.2.13. Let (T, Œ∑, ¬µ) be a monad on C. The canonical functor K : CT ‚ÜíCT from
the Kleisli category to the Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore category is full and faithful and its image
consists of the free T-algebras.

‚ñ°
The upshot of Lemma 5.2.13 is that the Kleisli category for a monad embeds as the
full subcategory of free T-algebras and all maps between such. Lemma 5.2.13 also tells us
precisely when the Kleisli and Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore categories are equivalent: this is the case
when all algebras are free. For instance, all vector spaces are free modules over any chosen
basis, so the Kleisli and Eilenberg‚ÄìMoore categories for the free vector space monad are
equivalent. Another equivalence of this form, for the maybe monad on Set, appears in
disguise in Example 1.5.6.

5.4. Canonical presentations via free algebras
The reason for our particular interest in monadic functors, such as U : Ab ‚ÜíSet, is
that the forgetful functor UT : CT ‚ÜíC associated to the category of algebras for a monad T
on C has certain special properties, which are enumerated in Propositions 5.4.3 and 5.4.9,
Lemma 5.6.1, Theorem 5.6.5, and Proposition 5.6.11. These properties are transferred
along the equivalence to any monadic functor. The Ô¨Årst of these, discussed in this section
and used in the next to prove the monadicity theorem, demonstrate that any algebra for a
monad admits a canonical presentation as a quotient of free algebras.

Ad hoc presentations as described by (5.4.2) can be a useful way to deÔ¨Åne abelian
groups, but they are unlikely to be functorial: a homomorphism œï: A ‚ÜíA‚Ä≤ is unlikely
to carry the chosen presentation for A to sets of generators and relations for A‚Ä≤. There is,
however, a canonical‚Äîby which we mean functorial‚Äîpresentation of any abelian group.
Rather than choose a proper subset of generators, we take all of the elements of A to be
generators; the canonical evaluation homomorphism Œ±: Z[A] ‚Ü†A is certainly surjective.
Similarly, rather than choose any particular set of relations in Z[A], we take all of the
elements of Z[A] to be ‚Äúrelations.‚Äù Here we do not intend to send every formal sum of
elements of A to zero; the result would be the trivial group. Instead, we generalize the
meaning of presentation, making use of the fact that coequalizers are more Ô¨Çexible than
cokernels. Proposition 5.4.3 will demonstrate that the diagram
Z[Z[A]]
¬µA
/
Z[Œ±] / Z[A]
Œ± / / A
is always a coequalizer. That is, any abelian group is the quotient of the free abelian group
on its underlying set modulo the relation that identiÔ¨Åes a formal sum with the element to
which it is evaluated.

Lemma 5.4.6. The underlying fork of a split coequalizer diagram is a coequalizer. More-
over, it is an absolute colimit: any functor preserves this coequalizer.
Proof. Given a map k: y ‚Üíw so that k f = kg, we must show that k factors through
h; uniqueness of a hypothetical factorization follows because h is a split epimorphism. The
factorization is given by the map ks: z ‚Üíw, as demonstrated by the following easy diagram
chase:
ksh = k ft = kgt = k .
Now clearly split coequalizers, which are just diagrams of a particular shape, are
preserved by any functor, so the equationally-witnessed universal property of the underlying
fork is also preserved by any functor.

5.5. Recognizing categories of algebras
There are many versions of the monadicity theorem, which characterizes those adjunc-
tions for which objects in the domain of the right adjoint can be regarded as algebras for
the monad deÔ¨Åned on the codomain. The one presented here is due to Beck [Bec67].
Theorem 5.5.1 (monadicity theorem). A right adjoint functor U : D ‚ÜíC is monadic if
and only if it creates coequalizers of U-split pairs.
Recalling DeÔ¨Ånition 5.4.8, the meaning of this result is the following.

There is a unifying term that encompasses each of the categories listed in Corollary
5.5.3.
Definition 5.5.4. A functor is Ô¨Ånitary if it preserves Ô¨Åltered colimits.
In particular, a monad T : C ‚ÜíC is Ô¨Ånitary if it preserves Ô¨Åltered colimits in C. If a
right adjoint is Ô¨Ånitary, then so is its monad because its left adjoint preserves all colimits.
Definition 5.5.5. A category A is a category of models for an algebraic theory if there
is a Ô¨Ånitary monadic functor U : A ‚ÜíSet.
Any category of models for an algebraic theory is locally Ô¨Ånitely presentable, in the
sense of DeÔ¨Ånition 4.6.16 [AR94, 2.78]. Another familiar category is monadic over sets,
though it is not a model of an algebraic theory because its monad is not Ô¨Ånitary.
Corollary 5.5.6. The underlying set functor U : cHaus ‚ÜíSet from the category of
compact HausdorÔ¨Äspaces is monadic.
The following proof is due to Par√© [Par71]. As in the proof of Corollary 5.5.3, a key
point is that split coequalizers are absolute coequalizers, preserved by any functor.
Proof. A topological space may be deÔ¨Åned to be a set X equipped with a closure
operator (‚àí): PX ‚ÜíPX on its set of subsets satisfying four axioms for all A, B ‚ààPX:
(i) ‚àÖ= ‚àÖ,
(ii) A ‚äÇA,
(iii) A = A,
(iv) A ‚à™B = A ‚à™B .
A function f : X ‚ÜíY between topological spaces is continuous if and only if f(A) ‚äÇf(A)
for all A ‚äÇX and closed11 if and only if f(A) = f(A). All continuous functions between
compact HausdorÔ¨Äspaces are closed; that is, a function f : X ‚ÜíY between compact
HausdorÔ¨Äspaces lifts to a map in cHaus if and only if it preserves the closure operator.

are reÔ¨Çexive directed graphs, with speciÔ¨Åed ‚Äúidentity‚Äù endoarrows of each vertex.
The construction of the inverse equivalence to the canonical functor K : D ‚ÜíCT
given in the proof of Theorem 5.5.1 can be adapted to various other hypotheses on the
given right adjoint U : D ‚ÜíC.
In the categorical literature, these variant theorems
are traditionally assigned three-letter acronyms. Theorem 5.5.1 is sometimes called PTT,
‚Äúprecise tripleability13 theorem,‚Äù ‚Äúprecise‚Äù because the characterization involves a condition
that is necessary, as well as suÔ¨Écient. Statements of the ‚Äúcrude tripleability theorem‚Äù (CTT)
and ‚Äúvulgar tripleability theorem‚Äù (VTT) can be found in the exercises to [ML98a, ¬ßVI.7].
Another variant is the ‚ÄúreÔ¨Çexive tripleability theorem‚Äù:
Proposition 5.5.8 (RTT). If U : D ‚ÜíC has a left adjoint and if
(i) D has coequalizers of reÔ¨Çexive14 pairs,
(ii) U preserves coequalizers of reÔ¨Çexive pairs, and
(iii) U reÔ¨Çects isomorphisms,
then U is monadic.
Proof. Exercise 5.5.iii.

5.6. Limits and colimits in categories of algebras
Recall that a category A is monadic over C if there is an adjunction

coequalizers of U-split pairs, a somewhat strange technical condition that can be relatively
practical to check. Our aim in this section is to present a few results from categorical
universal algebra, which describe some of the common features of categories that are
equivalent to categories of algebras. In particular, these properties hold for Set‚àó, Monoid,
Group, Ab, Ring, CRing, ModR, Affk, SetBG, Lattice, and cHaus, all of which are monadic
over Set,15 and sometimes fail for Poset, Top, and Field, which are not.16

Corollary 5.6.4. Any bijective homomorphism in a category of models for an algebraic
theory is an isomorphism.
For instance, the inverse of a bijective homomorphism between groups is also a ho-
momorphism. This, of course, is not diÔ¨Écult to prove. Corollary 5.6.4 eliminates the
redundancy of proving the same result over and over again in many similar contexts.
The contrapositive to Lemma 5.6.1 demonstrates that the categories Top or Poset are not
monadic over sets: there exist non-invertible maps that act as the identity on underlying
sets.

An immediate corollary completes the unÔ¨Ånished business of Proposition 4.5.15.
Corollary 5.6.6. The inclusion of a reÔ¨Çective subcategory creates all limits. In particular,
a reÔ¨Çective subcategory of a complete category is complete.
Proof. Proposition 5.3.3 proves that the inclusion of a reÔ¨Çective subcategory is
monadic. By Theorem 5.6.5(i), it follows that this inclusion creates all limits that ex-
ist in the codomain.

Another corollary is the completeness result promised in ¬ß3.5.
Corollary 5.6.7. Any category that is monadic over Set is complete, with limits created
by the monadic forgetful functor.
Proof. Theorem 3.2.6 proves that Set is complete. Theorem 5.6.5 demonstrates that
any category that is monadic over Set is also complete.

Corollary 5.6.9. Set is cocomplete.

In fact, Ab and any category of models for an algebraic theory is cocomplete. However,
the construction of those colimits that are not preserved by the monad is more complicated.
To explore this problem, consider the monadic forgetful functor U : Group ‚ÜíSet. Both
Set and Group admit coproducts; in Set these are simply disjoint unions, while in Group
they are given by the free product. The free product of groups G and H is the group
G ‚àóH of words whose letters are drawn from the elements of G and H together with formal
inverses, modulo relations deÔ¨Åned using the group operations in each group. Note, in
particular, that U : Group ‚ÜíSet does not preserve (and so, in particular, does not create)
coproducts. However, there is a more precise description of the free product G ‚àóH that can
be stated entirely in terms of the free ‚ä£forgetful adjunction
Set
F
/
‚ä•
Group ,
U
o
which generalizes to deÔ¨Åne coproducts relative to any monadic adjunction.
A Ô¨Årst approximation to the free product G ‚àóH is given by F(UG ‚äîUH), the free
group on the disjoint union of the underlying sets of G and H. Elements of this group are
words whose letters are elements of G or H, but it remains to impose the relations generated
by words in G and words in H. These words in G and words in H are elements of the
set UFUG ‚äîUFUH, deÔ¨Åned to be the disjoint union of the underlying sets of the free
groups on the underlying sets of G and H. The free group on this set F(UFUG ‚äîUFUH)
is the group of words of words in the letters G and H so that each subword is exclusively
comprised of letters drawn from a single one of these groups. The desired relations that
deÔ¨Åne G ‚àóH as a quotient of F(UG ‚äîUH) identify a word of words of this type with the
word obtained by evaluating each subword using the group structure of G or of H.
To encode the desired relations, we deÔ¨Åne a natural pair of group homomorphisms.

CHAPTER 6
All Concepts are Kan Extensions
The notion of Kan extensions subsumes all the
other fundamental concepts of category theory.
Saunders Mac Lane, Categories for the Working
Mathematician [ML98a]
Extension problems are pervasive in mathematics. For instance, iterated products of
a Ô¨Åxed positive real number, such as 2, deÔ¨Åne its natural number powers. The resulting
function 2‚àí: N ‚ÜíR>0 can be extended to a homomorphism from the additive group of
integers to the multiplicative group of non-negative reals by declaring 2‚àín B ( 1
2)n for n ‚ààN.
The resulting function 2‚àí: Z ‚ÜíR>0 can be extended further to an additive homomorphism
deÔ¨Åned on the rationals by declaring 2
1
n B
n‚àö
2 for n ‚ààN. Finally, 2‚àí: Q ‚ÜíR>0 can
be extended to an order-preserving function on the reals, not via any explicitly described
arithmetic formula, but by using the fact that R>0 has limits of all bounded increasing
sequences.
The construction of the function 2‚àí: R ‚ÜíR>0 as an order-preserving function ex-
tending 2‚àí: Q ‚ÜíR>0 is a special case of a solution to a categorically-deÔ¨Åned problem
of extending a functor F : C ‚ÜíE along another functor K : C ‚ÜíD, possibly but not
necessarily a subcategory inclusion. In general, strict extensions may or may not exist.
Obstructions can take several forms: two arrows in C with distinct images in E might be
identiÔ¨Åed in D, or two objects in C might have a morphism between their images in D
but not in E. For this reason, it is more reasonable to ask for a ‚Äúbest approximation‚Äù to
an extension taking the form of a universal natural transformation pointing either from or
to F. The resulting categorical notion, quite simple to deÔ¨Åne, is surprisingly ubiquitous
throughout mathematics, as we shall soon discover.
Solutions to this categorical extension problem, the Ô¨Årst examples of which were studied
in [Kan58], are called Kan extensions and come in dual left-handed and right-handed forms.
The general deÔ¨Ånition and some basic examples of Kan extensions are introduced in ¬ß6.1,
which explains how Kan extensions deÔ¨Åne partial adjoints to the functor K‚àó: ED ‚ÜíEC
deÔ¨Åned by restriction along K : C ‚ÜíD. A formula, deÔ¨Åning left Kan extensions as certain
colimits and right Kan extensions as certain limits, is presented in ¬ß6.2, which in particular
provides criteria guaranteeing that certain Kan extensions exist.
In ¬ß6.3, we discover
that the (co)limit formulae imply that the Kan extensions so-deÔ¨Åned satisfy a stronger
universal property than was originally supposed: namely, Kan extensions deÔ¨Åned by the
(co)limit formulae are pointwise Kan extensions. In ¬ß6.4, we study total derived functors,
which are deÔ¨Åned as certain Kan extensions, and introduce an abstract general framework
that constructs ‚Äúpoint-set level‚Äù lifts of these derived functors. Unexpectedly‚Äîsince total
derived functors take values in homotopy categories, which have few limits and colimits‚Äî
the total derived functors constructed in this manner have the stronger universal property of
being pointwise and in fact absolute Kan extensions, a fact that has signiÔ¨Åcant consequence
189

190
6. ALL CONCEPTS ARE KAN EXTENSIONS
for derived adjunctions. We conclude in ¬ß6.5 by showing how simple special cases of Kan
extensions can be used to redeÔ¨Åne adjunctions, limits, colimits, and monads and generalize
the Yoneda lemma.
6.1. Kan extensions
The data of a Kan extension consists of a functor and a natural transformation satisfying
a universal property relative to other similar pairs. For the reasons discussed in ¬ß4.4, this
deÔ¨Ånition can also be stated for 2-categories other than the 2-category of categories, func-
tors, and natural transformations, in which case the eponym ‚ÄúKan‚Äù is frequently dropped.
Since we are only interested in this one example of a 2-category at present, we retain the
traditional name.

Example 6.2.7. Consider the posets (Q, ‚â§) of rationals and (R>0, ‚â§) of non-negative reals.
We have the functor 2‚àí: Q ‚ÜíR>0 deÔ¨Åned at the introduction to this chapter. The poset R>0
has all limits (inÔ¨Åma) and colimits (suprema) of bounded subsets. In particular, we may
use Theorem 6.2.1 to deÔ¨Åne the left Kan extension along Q ,‚ÜíR. The value of Lan 2x is
the supremum (the colimit) of all 2q with q ‚ààQ and q ‚â§x. This is the usual deÔ¨Ånition of
2x, so we conclude that the exponential function 2‚àí: R ‚ÜíR>0 is the left Kan extension of
2‚àí: Q ‚ÜíR>0 along the inclusion Q ,‚ÜíR.
In this case, the exponential function 2‚àí: R ‚ÜíR>0 is also the right Kan extension of
2‚àí: Q ‚ÜíR>0 along the inclusion Q ,‚ÜíR; this is because 2x is also the inÔ¨Åmum of 2q for
all q ‚ààQ with x ‚â§q. Exercise 6.2.ii invites the reader to explore why the left and right Kan
extensions coincide in this case.

The composite comonad on Set‚àÜop is skn, the functor that maps a simplicial set to its

functors. We leave the details to those readers familiar with simplicial sets.
Exercises.
Exercise 6.2.i. Directed graphs are functors from the category with two objects E, V and
a parallel pair of maps s, t: E ‚áíV to Set. A natural transformation between two such
functors is a graph homomorphism. The forgetful functor DirGraph ‚ÜíSet that maps a
graph to its set of vertices is given by restricting along the functor from the terminal category
1 that picks out the object V. Use Theorem 6.2.1 to compute left and right adjoints to this
forgetful functor.
Exercise 6.2.ii. Give conditions on a monotone function f : Q ‚ÜíR, meaning a functor
f : (Q, ‚â§) ‚Üí(R, ‚â§), so that the left and right Kan extensions along Q ,‚ÜíR exist and
coincide.
6.3. Pointwise Kan extensions
Unusually for a mathematical object deÔ¨Åned by a universal property, generic Kan
extensions are rather poorly behaved, particularly in the case of derived functors, which
are introduced in the next section. On account of this, in his much cited book on enriched
category theory, Kelly reserves the name ‚ÄúKan extension‚Äù for pairs satisfying the condition
to be introduced shortly, calling those of our DeÔ¨Ånition 6.1.1 ‚Äúweak‚Äù and writing:
Our present choice of nomenclature is based on our failure to Ô¨Ånd a
single instance where a weak Kan extension plays any mathematical
role whatsoever. [Kel82, ¬ß4]
By the categorical community‚Äôs consensus, the important Kan extensions are pointwise
Kan extensions. To explain this, a preliminary notion is needed:
3The category FieldE
F has very few limits but it does have the limits of Theorem 6.2.1 in this instance; see
Theorem 6.3.7.

Theorem 6.2.1 constructs certain left Kan extensions using colimits. As left adjoints
preserve colimits (Theorem 4.5.3), it follows that left adjoints preserve such left Kan
extensions. Indeed, left adjoints preserve all left Kan extensions, regardless of how they
are constructed:
Lemma 6.3.2. Left adjoints preserve left Kan extensions.
Proof 1. Suppose given a left Kan extension (LanK F, Œ∑) with codomain E and suppose
further that L: E ‚ÜíF has a right adjoint R with unit Œπ: 1E ‚áíRL and counit ŒΩ: LR ‚áí1F.
Then given H : D ‚ÜíF there are natural isomorphisms
FD(L LanK F, H)  ED(LanK F, RH)  EC(F, RHK)  FC(LF, HK) .
Taking H = L LanK F, these isomorphisms act on the identity natural transformation as
follows:

Hence, by the Yoneda lemma, (L LanK F, LŒ∑) is a left Kan extension of LF along K.

Example 6.3.3. The forgetful functor U : Top ‚ÜíSet has both left and right adjoints. It
follows from Lemma 6.3.2 that U preserves the left and right Kan extensions of Example
6.1.7, as constructed in Example 6.2.8.
Example 6.3.4. The forgetful functor U : Vectk ‚ÜíSet preserves limits, because it is a
right adjoint, but not colimits because the underlying set of a direct sum is not simply
the coproduct of the underlying sets of vectors. Indeed, it is clear from the construction
given in Example 6.2.8 that the underlying set of a G-representation induced from an H-
representation is not equal to the G-set induced from the underlying H-set. Lemma 6.3.2
implies, however, that the underlying set of the coinduced representation does agree with
the coinduced G-set from the underlying H-set.

6.4. Derived functors as Kan extensions
In this section, we explain how derived functors in homological algebra or algebraic
topology are deÔ¨Åned using Kan extensions. A derived functor is derived from a functor
whose domain is a category equipped with a speciÔ¨Åed class of morphisms called ‚Äúweak
equivalences‚Äù and whose codomain is either another category with weak equivalences or
an ordinary category, in which case the weak equivalences are taken to be the class of
isomorphisms.
We introduce two varieties of derived functors. The Ô¨Årst of these are the total derived
functors, which are deÔ¨Åned to be Kan extensions along ‚Äúlocalization‚Äù functors that formally
invert the weak equivalences. In practice, total derived functors are frequently accompanied
by point-set derived functors, which are lifts of total derived functors to weak-equivalence-
preserving functors between the original categories.
After stating these deÔ¨Ånitions, we give an abstract account of a commonly applied
construction that can be used to construct point-set derived functors. Unexpectedly, the
total derived functors that are constructed in this way are pointwise Kan extensions, despite
the fact that homotopy categories have notoriously few limits and colimits. In fact, the

6.4. DERIVED FUNCTORS AS KAN EXTENSIONS
205
universal property enjoyed by the derived functors that are deÔ¨Åned via this construction is
even stronger and has important implications to the theory of derived adjunctions, which
we discuss.
Throughout, we reserve the term ‚Äúweak equivalences‚Äù for a speciÔ¨Åed class of mor-
phisms in a category that contains all the identities and satisÔ¨Åes one further condition.
The idea, that the weak equivalences are like isomorphisms but weaker, is encoded by a
condition that the class of weak equivalences must satisfy:
Definition 6.4.1. A class of weak equivalences W is a class of morphisms in a category
that contains the identities and satisÔ¨Åes the 2-of-6 property: if for any composable triple
of arrows

Lemma 6.4.2. Any class of morphisms in a category that contains the identities and satisÔ¨Åes
the 2-of-6 property necessarily also contains the isomorphisms.
Proof. Exercise 6.4.i.
‚ñ°
The modern viewpoint, that one can ‚Äúdo homotopy theory‚Äù in any category with a
class of weak equivalences, explains the following terminology.
Definition 6.4.3. A homotopical category is a category equipped with a class of weak
equivalences. A functor between such categories is homotopical if it preserves the weak
equivalences.
Remark 6.4.4 (the universal property of the homotopy category). Any homotopical cat-
egory C has an associated homotopy category HoC characterized by the following uni-
versal property: the homotopy category HoC is initial among categories E equipped with
a homotopical functor C ‚ÜíE, that is, a functor that sends weak equivalences in C to
isomorphisms.6 In analogy with ring theory, the universal functor C ‚ÜíHoC is referred to
as a localization functor.
The universal property of the localization functor‚Äîpre-composition with C ‚ÜíHoC
induces a bijective correspondence between functors HoC ‚ÜíE and homotopical functors
C ‚ÜíE‚Äîalso has a 2-dimensional aspect. Pre-composition with the localization functor
also induces a bijection between natural transformations between functors HoC ‚ÜíE and
natural transformations between the corresponding homotopical functors C ‚ÜíE; see
Exercise 6.4.ii.
The following explicit construction of the homotopy category is due to Gabriel and
Zisman [GZ67, 1.1]. The category HoC has the same objects as C. Its morphisms are
equivalence classes of Ô¨Ånite zig-zags of morphisms in C, with only weak equivalences
permitted to go backward, modulo the following relations:
‚Ä¢ Adjacent arrows pointing in the same direction may be composed.
6Set-theoretic concerns make it somewhat tricky to specify what functor HoC represents. In the examples
of greatest interest, C is not small, in which case HoC is a priori not even locally small, though in practice it
frequently is.

‚Ä¢ Adjacent pairs

‚Ä¢ Identities pointing either forward or backward may be removed.
There is a canonical identity-on-objects localization functor

Example 6.4.5. When C has no speciÔ¨Åed subcategory of weak equivalences, as a default we
assign it the minimal homotopical structure, where the weak equivalences are precisely
the isomorphisms and the localization functor is the identity.
Example 6.4.6. There are two homotopical structures of interest on the category ChR of
chain complexes of R-modules. In the Ô¨Årst, the class of weak equivalences is taken to
be the chain homotopy equivalences. In the second, the class of weak equivalences is
enlarged to contain all quasi-isomorphisms, that is, chain maps f : A‚Ä¢ ‚ÜíB‚Ä¢ that induce
an isomorphism on graded abelian groups after applying the homology functor introduced
in Example 1.3.2(viii).
Example 6.4.7. The category of topological spaces also has two homotopical structures
of interest. In the Ô¨Årst, the class of weak equivalences is taken to be the class of homotopy
equivalences. In the second, this class is enlarged to contain all weak homotopy equiva-
lences, which are maps inducing isomorphisms on all homotopy groups. The homotopy
category of spaces is the homotopy category associated to the second homotopical struc-
ture. It is equivalent to the category HtpyCW of CW complexes and homotopy classes of
maps that appears in Examples 2.1.6(vi) and (vii).
It is in this context that we may deÔ¨Åne total derived functors.

There is a common setting in which derived functors exist and admit a simple con-
struction. Such categories have a collection of ‚Äúfat‚Äù objects on which the functor of interest
becomes homotopical and a functorial reÔ¨Çection into this full subcategory. The details are
encoded in the following axiomatization due to [DHKS04].
Definition 6.4.10.
‚Ä¢ A left deformation on a homotopical category C consists of an endofunctor Q together
with a natural weak equivalence q: Q
‚àº=‚áí1C.
‚Ä¢ A left deformation for a functor F : C ‚ÜíD between homotopical categories consists
of a left deformation (Q, q) for C such that F preserves all weak equivalences between
objects in a full subcategory containing the image of Q.
There is an obvious dual notion of right deformation r: 1C
‚àº=‚áíR.
Interestingly, left deformations can be used to construct point-set left derived functors,
a homotopical universal property that is independent of the deformation used.

‚ñ°
Unusually for constructions characterized by a universal property, generic total derived
functors are poorly behaved: for instance, the composite of the total left derived functors of
a pair of composable functors is not necessarily a total left derived functor for the composite.
The deÔ¨Åning universal property is also insuÔ¨Écient to prove that the total left derived functor
of a left adjoint is left adjoint to the total right derived functor of its right adjoint. The
problem with the standard deÔ¨Ånition is that total derived functors are not typically required
to be pointwise Kan extensions. In light of Theorem 6.3.7, this seems reasonable because
homotopy categories have few limits and colimits.7
Somewhat surprisingly, the particular construction of total derived functors via de-
formations given in Proposition 6.4.11 implies that the total derived functors produced in
this manner are pointwise and indeed absolute Kan extensions: not only are these Kan
extensions preserved by representables but they are preserved by all functors.
Proposition 6.4.12. The total left derived functor of a left deformable functor is an absolute
right Kan extension, and in particular pointwise.
Proof. If F : C ‚ÜíD is left deformable, it has a total left derived functor (Œ¥FQ, Œ¥Fq)
constructed using a left deformation (Q, q) for F and the localization functor Œ¥: D ‚ÜíHoD.
Because Kan extensions are characterized by a universal property, any total derived functor
of F is isomorphic to this one. To show that this right Kan extension is absolute, our task
is to show that for any functor H : HoD ‚ÜíE, the pair (HŒ¥FQ, HŒ¥Fq) again deÔ¨Ånes a right
Kan extension.
Note that (Q, q) also deÔ¨Ånes a left deformation for HŒ¥F, simply because the functor
H : HoD ‚ÜíE preserves isomorphisms. The proof of Proposition 6.4.11 now demonstrates
that (HŒ¥FQ, HŒ¥Fq) is a right Kan extension, as claimed.
‚ñ°
7With the exception of products and coproducts, the so-called ‚Äúhomotopy limits‚Äù and ‚Äúhomotopy colimits‚Äù do
not deÔ¨Åne limits and colimits in the homotopy category.

6.5. ALL CONCEPTS

In homological algebra or homotopy theory, deformable functors often come in adjoint
pairs, with the left adjoint admitting a left deformation and the right adjoint admitting a right
deformation. In such cases, the total derived functors are again adjoints, but interestingly
the proof requires Proposition 6.4.12: the mere universal property of being a left or right
Kan extension is not enough.
This is the main theorem from a paper of Maltsiniotis
[Mal07]. As with many non-trivial discoveries in category theory, once the statement is
known, the proof is elementary enough to leave to the reader:
Proposition 6.4.13. Suppose F ‚ä£G is an adjunction between homotopical categories and
suppose also that F has a total left derived functor LF, G has a total right derived functor
RG, and both derived functors are absolute Kan extensions. Then the total derived functors
form an adjunction LF ‚ä£RG between the homotopy categories.
Proof. Exercise 6.4.iv.

Finally, we turn to monads. We have seen that a right adjoint functor G: D ‚ÜíC
induces a monad on C whose endofunctor is the composite of G with its left adjoint. More
generally, assuming C has suÔ¨Écient limits, any functor G: D ‚ÜíC induces a monad on C.
If G has a left adjoint F, this monad is the monad GF but this construction applies more
generally.

Example 6.5.13. The Giry monad of Example 5.1.5(iv) is also a codensity monad for the
inclusion of a subcategory of C ,‚ÜíMeas. The objects of C are the Ô¨Ånite powers of the
unit interval I = [0, 1] ‚äÇR, equipped with the Borel œÉ-algebra, plus the measurable space
of sequences in I that converge to 0. The objects can be regarded as convex subspaces of
Euclidean space, from which perspective the morphisms are deÔ¨Åned to be all aÔ¨Éne maps
between these spaces. Omitting the space of sequences, the subcategory of Ô¨Ånite powers
of the interval and aÔ¨Éne maps generates a variant of the Giry monad for Ô¨Ånitely additive
probability measures as its codensity monad. See [Ave16].
